Although robust π–π stacking is beneficial for cross-layer electron transport, it limits the adequate exposure of CoN4 active sites to some extent. The synthesis of Co(II)-metalated 3D COFs can spatially reduce the stacking of cobalt-phthalocyanine to increase the accessibility of CO2 to CoN4. Following this approach, Jiang et al. synthesized CoPc–PI-COF-3 with a pts topology and it possessed an adsorption capacity of 21 cm3 g−1 for CO2 at 298 K and 1.0 bar.85 Although its conductivity of 0.21 mS m−1 is an order of magnitude lower than that of the common 2D phthalocyanine-COFs, resulting in its slightly higher Tafel slope (117 mV dec−1) and much larger charge transfer resistance, its CO2RR activity is satisfactory due to the full exposure of the CoN4 active sites. A maximum faradaic efficiency of 96% with a partial current density for CO of 31.7 mA m−2 was obtained for CoPc–PI-COF-3 at −0.9 V. In addition, the CoPc–PI-COF-3 electrode exhibited excellent long-term durability, maintaining a faradaic efficiency of more than 90% with TON and TOF of 35500 and 0.6 s−1 respectively, for 20 h of continuous running at −0.8 V. Only 0.18% of cobalt leaked from the electrode into the electrolyte.
Rhenium–carbonyl complexes are well-established electrocatalysts for the reduction of CO2 to CO, but no additional advantage was gained by combining them with COFs.239 The faradaic efficiency of COF-2,2′-bpy-Re (81%) was lower than that of the corresponding molecular catalyst (99%). Even more unfortunately, the CO2RR activity of COF-2,2′-bpy-Re significantly decreased after 30 min of controlled potential electrolysis, because of its poor electrical conductivity, chemical stability, and mass transfer kinetics. Fortunately, the immobilization of manganese–carbonyl complexes in COFs strongly enhances the CO2RR activity. In 2021, Lloret-Fillol et al. prepared a Mn(I)-metalated COF as a CO2RR electrocatalyst by grafting Mn(CO)5Br on COFbpyvia CO–bipyridine ligand exchange.246 The COFbpyMn|NT composite, composed of COFbpyMn and multiwalled carbon nanotubes, exhibits higher CO2RR activity than the corresponding molecular electrocatalyst composite fac-Mn(2,2′-bipyridine)(CO)3Br|NT. In 0.5 M NaHCO3 solution, the CO faradaic efficiency of COFbpyMn|NT was 62% under optimized conditions, while that of fac-Mn(2,2′-bipyridine)(CO)3Br|NT was only 13%, and the TOF of COFbpyMn|NT was 627 h−1, which was 15.7 times higher than that of fac-Mn(2,2′-bipyridine)(CO)3Br|NT. Spectroelectrochemical characterization showed that Mn(–I) is a key intermediate in CO2RR, while the Mn(0)–Mn(0) dimer negatively affects CO2RR. Due to the structural limitation of COFbpyMn, the Mn(0)–Mn(0) dimer cannot be formed within the framework, leading to improved CO2RR activity.
Copper-complexes are a class of heterogeneous CO2RR electrocatalysts with a tendency to produce hydrocarbons, alcohols, and carboxylic acids.554 Copper-metalated COFs were explored for CO2RR to obtain methane. In 2022, Lan et al. synthesized multi-dimensional superstructures of COF-366-Cu as CO2RR catalysts based on copper-porphyrin.555 The CH4 faradaic efficiencies of the COF-366-Cu hollow sphere, nanofiber, and nanosheet are 68.2, 71, and 64.2% respectively, with CH4 partial current densities of 194.7, 248.6, and 177.3 mA cm−2 at a potential of −0.9 V. The electrochemical double-layer capacitances of the hollow sphere, nanofiber, and nanosheet are 2.07, 2.73, and 1.2 mF cm−2 respectively, while the charge transfer resistances are 6.51, 5.68, and 9.83 Ω respectively, indicating that morphology modification affects not only the contact between the electrolyte and the Cu(II) active sites, but also the transfer of electrons from the catalyst surface to CO2, thus affecting the CO2RR performance. It is important to mention that ethylene was not avoided in this CO2RR process, and the faradaic efficiencies for ethylene of the hollow sphere, nanofiber, and nanosheet were obtained as 14.6, 16.6, and 5.6%, respectively. Moreover, for all three COF-366-Cu morphologies, the CO2RR performance is better than that of their metal-free counterpart (COF-366) for which HER mainly occurs to obtain hydrogen. Similarly, the AAn-COF-Cu nanofiber obtained by immobilizing Cu(II) at the β-ketoenamine-linkage in AAn-COF provided a CH4 faradaic efficiency of 77% with CH4 partial current density of −128.1 mA cm−2 at a potential of −0.9 V under optimal conditions, while the ethylene faradaic efficiency dropped to less than 5%.556 However, HER is still present with an H2 faradaic efficiency of approximately 15%. Furthermore, the zinc-based analog AAn-COF-Zn mainly catalyzes CO2RR to CO and HER to H2, and the cobalt-based analog AAn-COF-Co shows almost no CO2RR activity within the tested potential range.
Covalent attachment of basic groups to copper-metalated COFs to enhance CO2 uptake can inhibit competitive HER and improve CO2RR selectivity. As shown in Fig. 37A–C, Cu-Tph-COF-Dct modified with 4,6-diamino-1,3,5-triazin-2-yl groups on the pore wall exhibited enhanced CO2 adsorption (61.4 cm3 g−1, 273 K) and elevated CO2 adsorption enthalpy (25.9 kJ mol−1).557 In a KOH solution, Cu-Tph-COF-Dct exhibited a total current density of 537.4 mA cm−2 at −1.1 V that was higher than that of the unmodified Cu-Tph-COF-OH (394.2 mA cm−2), indicating enhanced electrocatalytic activity. At the optimal potential of −0.9 V, the faradaic efficiency of Cu-Tph-COF-Dct is approximately 80% for CH4 and is nearly 20% for H2, while the faradaic efficiency values for CO and ethylene are negligible. Mechanistic studies suggest that the amino and triazine N enhanced the uptake and activation of CO2 and promoted the reduction of CO2 to CO that was subsequently enriched near the Cu(II) centers and reduced to CH4.
 Fig. 37  Cu-Metalated COFs for CO2RR. (A) Nitrogen-rich Cu-Tph-COF-Dct for CO2RR to generate methane in a flow cell system. (B) Attenuated total reflection in situ FT-IR spectra of Cu-Tph-COF-Dct during the electrocatalytic CO2RR. (C) Faradaic efficiencies for CH4, H2, CO, and C2H4 at different applied potentials using Cu-Tph-COF-Dct as an electrocatalyst. Adapted with permission of ref. 557. Copyright © 2021 Wiley-VCH GmbH. (D) Structure of CTF-Cu-4.8%. (E) Aberration-corrected high-angle annular dark STEM image of CTF-Cu-4.8%. (F) Faradaic efficiencies for gas products during the CTF-Cu-4.8%-catalyzed CO2RR in CO2-saturated 0.3 M KCl solution at different potentials. SHE, standard hydrogen electrode. (G) Real-time Fourier-transformed EXAFS spectra of CTF-Cu-4.8% at −1.45 V. Adapted with permission of ref. 249. Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society. 
Careful design of the Cu coordination environment in COF-supported Cu electrocatalysts can enhance kinetic viability and improve product selectivity to obtain a wide variety of C2 products in aqueous solutions. In 2020, Yang et al. synthesized the Cu(II)-loaded COF of CTF-Cu-4.8% by post-synthetic metalation of the CTF-B COF, as shown in Fig. 37D–G.249 According to the XANES and EXAFS spectra, the oxidation state of Cu in CTF-Cu-4.8% was assigned as +2, and the coordination environment was designated as [CuN2Cl2]. In the H-shaped cell, the liquid products given by CTF-Cu-4.8% are formic acid and a small amount of acetic acid, and the gaseous products include CO, H2, methane, and ethylene. At a potential of −1.30 V, the main products are H2 and CO. As the potential increases, the faradaic efficiencies of CO and H2 gradually decrease, while the faradaic efficiency of hydrocarbons (i.e., methane and ethylene) increases. Specifically, faradaic efficiencies reach their respective maximum values of 30.6% at −1.47 V for ethylene and of 72.0% at −1.55 V for methane. Time-dependent XANES and EXAFS spectra indicated that during electrolysis, Cu(II) was reduced to form copper-clusters. The aggregated copper atom clusters confined within the CTF-B matrix are the true active sites of CO2RR. Notably, the size of the generated copper atom clusters is approximately 1.09 nm due to the steric confinement effect of CTF-B, which effectively avoided the formation of large nanoparticles and ensured excellent durability during CO2RR. Furthermore, CTF-Cu-4.8%-catalyzed CO electroreduction mainly produced ethylene and acetate with the maximum faradaic efficiencies of 35.0% for ethylene and 33.4% for acetate, but no formate was obtained. Not surprisingly, for the unmetalated CTF-B, only HER was observed, indicating the key role of Cu-metalation in CO2RR.
Although some progress has been made in replacing noble metal catalysts for electrocatalytic CO2RR, the application of metalated COFs in CO2RR is still in early stage. Taking full advantage of COFs to precisely control the active metal sites with desirable geometric distribution at the atomic level is a promising research direction in this field. In addition, the effect of the COF matrix on the CO2RR process is also worth investigating to fully understand the structure–performance relationship.


3.3.4. Electrocatalytic N2 reduction. 
Ammonia plays a crucial role in the production of fertilizers, dyes, and explosives. The Haber–Bosch process, an energy- and capital-intensive process for the synthesis of ammonia from hydrogen and nitrogen at high temperatures and pressures established in the early 20th century, is still the main method for the industrial production of ammonia.558 Electrocatalytic nitrogen reduction reaction (NRR) under ambient conditions is very attractive for achieving sustainable and flexible alternative to the Haber–Bosch process for ammonia production.559 To date, iron and titanium phthalocyanine-based COFs have been reported as potential NRR catalysts.
As reported by Feng and co-workers in 2021, in nitrogen-saturated 0.01 M H2SO4, the iron phthalocyanine-based FePc-pz COF gave an ammonia faradaic efficiency of 31.9% and an ammonia yield of 33.6 μg (h mg)−1 at −0.1 V.148 The FeN4 active sites in the FePc-pz COF readily adsorbed N2 and the π-back interaction between the 3d electrons of Fe(II) and the π* antibonding orbital of N2 led to N2 activation. In addition, the CoPc-pz COF induced effective N2 adsorption and activation as well, but its high HER activity greatly inhibited NRR. The N2 adsorption of the MPc-pz COF (M = Mn, Ni, Cu, Zn) is weaker than that of the FePc-pz COF, thus providing poor NRR activity with NH3 faradaic efficiencies below 10%.
One year later, Jin et al. reported the NRR activity of Ti-metalated COFs.560 In N2-saturated 0.05 M HCl, TiPc-COF provided a maximum NH3 faradaic efficiency of 34.6% at −0.4 V and a maximum NH3 yield of 27 μg (h mg)−1 at −0.7 V. The TiN4 sites in TiPc-COF favor adsorption of N2 rather than H+, thus inhibiting HER. The interaction of 3d electrons of Ti with the π* antibonding orbitals of N2 enables N2 activation, which is beneficial for NRR.
In addition, Su et al. screened 20 metallophthalocyanine-COFs by density functional theory calculations, suggesting that a molybdenum phthalocyanine-based COF may be the most efficient NRR catalyst, in which the hybridization of Mo 4d and N2 2p orbitals plays an important role in promoting NRR.561 Sarkar et al. reported similar results showing that Mo-salphen-COF has a theoretical faradaic efficiency of 41% for NRR based on density functional theory calculations.562

3.4. Photocatalysis
Efforts to develop clean energy sources have remained continuous due to the depletion of traditional energy sources and the environmental pollution problems they cause.563 Solar energy, an abundant renewable energy source, is one of the most promising renewable energy sources for replacing fossil fuels and mitigating the global energy crisis.564 Solar-driven chemical processes, including water splitting, CO2 reduction, pollutant degradation, and organic reactions, have attracted great research interest.
In 2014, Lotch et al. reported the first example of the HER using COFs under visible light with platinum as a co-catalyst.565 Since this pioneering work, COFs have been extensively developed for photocatalysis.125,566 In general, photocatalytic reactions induced by COFs involve four main steps: first, the COF absorbs light with energy greater than the band gap; then, electrons in the valence band are excited to the conduction band, while the corresponding holes are formed in the valence band; next, the electrons and holes (collectively called photogenerated carriers) migrate to the COF surface; and finally, the electrons induce reduction reactions, while the holes induce oxidation reactions.567
COFs are ideal platforms for photocatalysts, offering some distinct advantages.568 First, COFs are synthesized from predesigned monomers, thus allowing for the integration of specific functions. Second, the adjustable pore metrics and high crystallinity of COFs help to suppress the charge recombination of the photoexcited states. Third, the π-conjugated structure within the COF-layers and the π-electron columns between the layers enable high charge carrier mobility. In addition, the high stability of COFs can effectively prevent photodegradation and performance degradation during long irradiation.569 All these advantages endow COFs with great potential for application in photocatalysis.570
The application of metalated COFs in photocatalysis is attracting attention.567 In addition to directly catalyzing photoreactions, the incorporated metal ions can adjust the band gap and visible-light absorption to modulate the optical properties of COFs. For example, the embedded Pt nanoparticles can be the co-catalysts to enable recovery and reuse, while Ag(I) and Fe(III) can be the OER sacrificial agents.571 Furthermore, conventional semiconducting photocatalytic materials, such as CdS,572–574 TiO2,364,575 Bi2WO6,576 and Ni(OH)2,577 can be combined with COFs to form heterojunctions, promoting the transfer of photogenerated charge carriers, inhibiting electron–hole recombination, and optimizing the photocatalytic activities of the resulting materials.578 All these features empower metalated COFs as functionally oriented multifunctional platforms for designing efficient photocatalysts.
In this section, we will introduce the applications of metalated COFs in photocatalysis, including photocatalytic HER and CO2RR, pollutant removal, and organic transformations.

3.4.1. Photocatalytic hydrogen evolution. 
Photocatalytic HER based on a ternary system of semiconductors, cocatalysts, and sacrificial agents is an effective means of converting solar energy into hydrogen energy.579
Due to their broad light absorption capacity, porphyrin-COFs have been shown to be good photocatalysts. Embedding different metal ions into porphyrin units can tune the photophysical properties and thus optimize the photocatalytic activity. In 2021, Wang et al. reported the synthesis of acylhydrazone-linked MPor-DETH-COFs (M = Co, Ni, Zn) with metalloporphyrins and their HER activities (Fig. 38).580 With the aid of a co-catalyst (Pt) and a sacrificial agent (triethanolamine), the generated MPor-DETH-COFs (M = Co, Ni, Zn) splitted water to produce hydrogen under visible-light irradiation, and their catalytic performance increased in the order of Co, Ni, and Zn. Of note, MPor-DETH-COFs (M = Co, Ni, Zn) have similar optical band gaps (1.82–1.88 eV) but vastly different charge carrier dynamics. The 3d7 Co(II) in CoPor-DETH-COF leads to significant porphyrin-to-metal charge transfer, which inhibits photogenerated charge carrier migration, resulting in the worst HER performance. While the 3d10 Zn(II) strictly forbids ligand-to-metal charge transfer, the photogenerated holes of ZnPor-DETH-COF can smoothly migrate to the surface through the porphyrin-on-porphyrin column, and the electrons can migrate to the surface along the Zn-on-Zn column, which results in a long excited-state charge separation lifetime. Ultimately, the photogenerated electrons migrate to the Pt co-catalyst for the HER to generate hydrogen, while the holes are quenched by the triethanolamine sacrificial agent.
 Fig. 38  Metalloporphyrin-based MPor-DETH-COF (M = Co, Ni, Zn) for photocatalytic HER. (A) Schematic illustrations of photogenerated charge carrier transport. (B) Frontier orbital energies of MPor-DETH-COF (M = Co, Ni, Zn). (C) HER rates of MPor-DETH-COF (M = Co, Ni, Zn) in phosphate buffer solution with the help of H2PtCl6 and triethanolamine under a Xe lamp (300 W, >400 nm). 
Metal ions bound to COF-scaffolds can also regulate charge carrier separation and transfer. In 2022, Yu et al. reported a Zn(II)-involved hollow COF cage of Zn@H-TpPa for HER under visible-light irradiation.581 Thereinto, Zn(II) enhances the polarity of the framework that is favorable for photogenerated charge separation. Moreover, Zn(II) causes the upward shift of both conduction and valence bands in H-TpPa, thus increasing the reduction potential of photogenerated electrons and promoting H2O reduction. In addition, the hollow morphology of Zn@H-TpPa facilitates mass transfer, which also enhances the HER activity. As a result, Zn@H-TpPa achieves an HER rate as high as 28 mmol (g h)−1 in phosphate buffered saline upon visible-light irradiation under the given conditions.
Pt is widely used as a co-catalyst for the HER due to its low overpotential and effective formation of Schottky junctions on COF surfaces.582 Typically, the H2PtCl6 precursor in the photoreaction system is in situ reduced to Pt(0) for HER catalysis, which complicates the Pt recovery. Very recently, Zhang and co-workers designed reusable platinum single atoms that were anchored on the β-ketoenamine-linked TpPa-1 COF for photocatalytic water splitting.351 The resulting Pt single atoms in Pt1@TpPa-1 possessed the six-coordination chemical environment of [PtC3NCl2], which acted as the electron collector to improve photogenerated carrier transfer. As expected, Pt1@TpPa-1 was extremely catalytically active, with a high HER rate of 719 μmol (g h)−1 and a TOF of 19.5 h−1 under visible-light irradiation. Importantly, Pt1@TpPa-1 was durable over four recycling runs.
Much work has been done to try to replace expensive Pt co-catalysts with inexpensive metals such as Cu(II),583 Ni(II),584 and Co(II).250,585 The coordination of metal ions with COFs is not only feasible but also results in better recoverability than the direct addition of the corresponding inorganic salts to the HER system.
Furthermore, another effective way to reduce the recombination rate of photogenerated electron–hole pairs is to fabricate heterojunctions of COFs and semiconductors. The interfacial charge transfer between the two components in the heterojunction physically separates electron–hole pairs, thus enhancing the HER. For example, in 2021, Shi et al. reported a SnS2/TpPa-1-COF heterojunction prepared by the hydrothermal method.376 Its HER rate under xenon lamp irradiation (>420 nm) without an additional co-catalyst reached 2.47 × 103 μmol (g h)−1, which is superior to that of TpPa-1-COF with Pt co-catalyst. Mechanistically, after electron and hole pairs were generated by both TpPa-1-COF and SnS2 under light irradiation, photogenerated electrons were transferred from TpPa-1-COF to the SnS2 surface, while photogenerated holes were transferred in the opposite direction. As a result, electrons and holes accumulated on SnS2 for the reduction reaction of H2O and on TpPa-1-COF for the oxidation reaction of the sacrificial agent (vitamin C), respectively. In this way, the photogenerated electrons and holes were spatially separated, thereby enhancing the photocatalytic HER activity. Based on the same principle, in the MOF/COF heterostructure of Cu3(HHTP)2 and TpPa-1, the Cu3(HHTP)2 MOF provides low interfacial charge transfer resistance as an electron acceptor, thus enhancing the HER rate nearly 100-fold compared to TpPa-1 alone.586 Theoretically, COF-based heterojunction photocatalysts are rich in possibilities,573,587–589 and the compositions, bonding forms, and energy band positions are highly controllable,590 which provides more chances to design low-cost, highly active HER photocatalysts in the future.
The more relevant literature on metalated COF-based photocatalysts for the HER is summarized in Table 6 to facilitate comparison of their properties and to provide information for designing new materials.204,250,351,352,364,376,580,581,583,585,586,591,592

3.4.2. Photocatalytic CO2 reduction. 
Photocatalytic production of solar fuels using CO2 as a carbon source is a promising strategic goal for securing future sustainable energy supplies and addressing global climatic changes.593 Over the past half century, various photocatalysts, including TiO2,594 metal sulfides,595 layered double hydroxide,596etc., have been developed to optimize light capture, charge separation, and activation of CO2, but they still face deficiencies such as low efficiency, unsatisfactory economic viability, poor selectivity, and difficulty in recycling.597,598 COFs, as a class of highly customizable porous materials whose light absorption capacity, semiconductor properties, and mass transfer abilities can be optimized through the design of the structure, have shown promise in CO2RR.599 Metals such as Co, Ni, Re, and Ru have been used in the synthesis of metalated COFs to improve the photocatalytic CO2RR. The roles of metalation include, but are not limited to, providing active centers for CO2 capture and activation, altering the CO2 reduction products (e.g., CO, HCOOH, and CH3OH), and inhibiting the unintended HER.
Cobalt porphyrin-based COFs are attractive catalysts for photocatalytic CO2RR due to their broad absorption extending into the near-infrared region, the efficient adsorption of CO2 by Co(II), and the activation of CO2 by CoN4.600 For example, in the presence of vitamin C as the sacrificial agent and Ru(2,2′-bipyridine)3Cl2 as the photosensitizer, the imine-linked COF-367-Co nanosheets with a thickness of 1.15 nm reduced CO2 to CO in water at a high rate of 10162 μmol (g h)−1 under the irradiation of a xenon lamp equipped with a 420 nm high-pass filter.601 The ultrathin COF nanosheets are favourable for mass transfer and exposure of the active sites, resulting in 82-fold and 6-fold enhancements in activity and CO selectivity, respectively, over those of their bulk counterparts.
In addition, sp2C-linked COFs have a fully conjugated structure, which provides a channel for photogenerated electron transfer. As shown in Fig. 39, Ye et al. grafted Co(II) on a 2,2′-bipyridine-derived sp2C-COFdpy to prepare the photocatalyst for CO2RR.602 The absorbance edge of sp2C-COFdpy was located at 640 nm with a band gap of 2.03 eV, while after Co(II)-metalation, sp2C-COFdpy-Co had a wider absorption range than sp2C-COFdpy, and its absorbance edge was extended to 733 nm with a band gap of 1.83 eV. Thus, Co(II)-metalation enhanced the light absorption of the photocatalyst in the long wavelength region, which is conducive to the photocatalytic CO2RR. In water, sp2C-COFdpy-Co produced 6.01 mmol g−1 CO after 6 h upon visible-light irradiation, while the unmetalated sp2C-COFdpy yielded only 0.17 mmol g−1 CO. Femtosecond transient absorption spectroscopy showed that Co(II)-metalation effectively inhibited charge recombination and boosted intramolecular electron transfer, thus enhancing the CO2RR. Furthermore, Co(II)-metalation suppressed the HER. The pyridinyl group in sp2C-COFdpy was a good hydrogen evolution site, leading to an HER rate of 670 μmol (g h)−1 under the given conditions, whereas the electron transfer from N to Co in sp2C-COFdpy-Co made it difficult for electrons to transfer to H2O, resulting in an HER rate of sp2C-COFdpy-Co of only 1/3 of that of sp2C-COFdpy. Ultimately, with enhanced absorption, fast photogenerated electron transfer, and suppressed HER, the selectivity of sp2C-COFdpy-Co to CO was as high as 81.4%, while that of sp2C-COFdpy was only 1.5%. Based on a similar design, Co-FPy-CON nanosheets also provide highly dispersed active CoN4 sites for the CO2RR, effectively reducing CO2 to CO under visible light irradiation with the assistance of an Ir(III)-complex as the photosensitizer and triethanolamine as the electron donor.603
 Fig. 39  Olefin-bridged COFs for CO2 photoreduction to CO in water. (A) Structure of sp2C-COFdpy-Co. (B) Solid-state UV-vis DRS. Inset: Photographs of sp2C-COFdpy-Co and sp2C-COFdpy dispersed in water. (C) Recycling performance of sp2C-COFdpy-Co. Samples were recovered every 18 h of illumination and used for the next photocatalytic experiment. (D) Proposed mechanism of photocatalytic CO2RR. Adapted with permission of ref. 602. Copyright © 2020 Elsevier B.V. 
The same year, Wang et al. studied the effect of different metals on the CO2RR.186 By comparing the performances of the tetraphenylene/metalloporphyrin coexistent NiP-TPE-COF and CoP-TPE-COF, it was found that Co-metalation significantly enhanced the catalytic activity, but Ni-metalation improved the CO2-to-CO selectivity. The reason for this difference is that CoN4 has a higher affinity for CO2 than NiN4, while NiP-TPE-COF has a higher propensity to capture protons for the proton transfer reduction of adsorbed CO2 than CoP-TPE-COF. A similar finding was obtained by Zou et al. using metallo-2,2′-bipyridine-COFs as a model, with Ni-TpBpy exhibiting 96% CO selectivity, higher than the 41% CO selectivity of Co-TpBpy.604 This study highlighted that 2,2′-bipyridine, besides as a chelating unit for Ni(II), makes additional contributions to improving the catalytic activity and selectivity of CO2 reduction by facilitating CO2 activation and inhibiting competitive H2 evolution. Inspired by this insight into the structure–property relationship, in 2022, Wang et al. further investigated the CO2RR activity of Ni(II)-metalated COFs with different coordination linkages.605 H-COF-Ni containing β-ketoenamine–Ni(II) has a higher CO yield (5694 vs. 1960 μmol g−1 in 2 h) and slightly lower selectivity (87 vs. 93%) than I-COF-Ni with imine–Ni(II). The coordination environment provided by β-ketoenamine linkage was more favorable for CO2 adsorption, activation, and photoreduction than that of imine linkage, leading to a higher CO2RR performance of H-COF-Ni than I-COF-Ni. Although imine could promote the transfer of photogenerated electrons along the nickel bridge between the I-COF-Ni layers, the separation and transfer of photogenerated carriers did not play a decisive role in the CO2RR in this system.
Since their discovery in the late 20th century, fac-chlorotricarbonyl(2,2′-bipyridine)rhenium(I) and its substituted bipyridine analogs have been known for their high CO2 reduction efficiency606 but are difficult to recover and reuse due to their homogeneous nature. In contrast, anchoring rhenium bipyridine carbonyl complexes on COFs is expected to overcome this deficiency and promises better photoreaction performance. In 2018, Huang et al. reported a COFBPDA-TTA with incorporated Re(2,2′-bipyridine)(CO)3Cl for CO2 photoreduction.141 The presence of effective electron transfer from the COFBPDA-TTA skeleton to the loaded Re(2,2′-bipyridine)(CO)3Cl moiety within Re-COFBPDA-TTA significantly inhibited charge recombination and distinctly promoted the adsorption and activation of CO2 at the Re(I) active sites. With triethanolamine as the sacrificial agent, Re-COFBPDA-TTA provided a CO yield of 15 mmol g−1 for 20 h with 98% CO selectivity upon visible-light irradiation. It is worth mentioning that the control experiments with isotope-labelled 13CO2 as the carbon source only yielded 13CO, and no 12CO was detected, confirming that the CO indeed came from the CO2 reduction rather than from the Re(I) carbonyl complex. Based on this design strategy, the Re(2,2′-bipyridine)(CO)3Cl-loaded sp2C-linked Bpy-sp2C-COF was also prepared, and it had a wide absorbance range and high CO2 affinity.251 Upon illumination, there was charge transfer from the pyrene unit to the Re(I)-complex along the Re-Bpy-sp2C-COF scaffold, forming long-range electron–hole pairs in which the holes are quenched by a sacrificial agent, while the electrons participate in CO2 reduction. In this way, Re-Bpy-sp2C-COF produced CO in acetonitrile at a rate of 1040 μmol (g h)−1 with a selectivity of 81%, using triethanolamine as a sacrificial agent and a proton source. Furthermore, the additional supplementation of an Ir(III)-complex as a blue light photosensitizer enabled the rate of the CO2RR induced by Re-Bpy-sp2C-COF to be further increased to 1400 μmol (g h)−1 under optimized conditions due to the occurrence of electron transfer between the Ir(III)-complex and Re-Bpy-sp2C-COF via oxidative quenching. Overall, the immobilization of the Re(I)–bipyridine complex in the COF-scaffolds is a highly feasible strategy for optimizing photocatalytic CO2RR activity, which was initially understood through effective intramolecular charge transfer. Zheng et al. recently further rationalized this strategy by investigating ultrafast charge transfer dynamics in such Re(I)-metalated COFs via transient optical spectroscopy and time-dependent density-functional theory calculations. In particular, they emphasized that upon excitation with high-energy photons, the photogenerated electrons located at the highly excited level of Re(I) have a long lifetime; some long-lived electrons that are transferred back from Re(I) are also retained on the COF-scaffold. Both factors contribute significantly to the two-electron reduction of CO2, which did not receive enough attention in previous studies.
As mentioned earlier, formally, CO2RR is a process in which CO2 is reduced by sacrificial agents under illumination. Therefore, in most cases, sacrificial agents such as triethanolamine, vitamin C, and triethylamine need to be added to the CO2RR system to consume the photogenerated holes. Such sacrificial agents not only waste the oxidation potential of photogenerated holes but also increase the cost of the reaction. In 2019, Lan et al. reported a photocatalytic CO2RR that used water as the electron donor; this reaction could simultaneously yield CO and O2 by coupling the CO2RR with the OER.187 Electron-deficient metalloporphyrins and an electron-rich tetrathiafulvalene were selected as monomers for the construction of COFs, respectively responsible for the formation of photogenerated electrons and holes for CO2 reduction and water oxidation (Fig. 40A). The resulting TTCOF-M (M = Ni, Zn, Cu) had different energy band structures, where the energy bands of TTCOF-Zn and TTCOF-Cu spanned the reduction potential of CO/CO2 and the oxidation potential of O2/H2O and could utilize H2O for CO2 reduction (Fig. 40B). As expected, under visible light, TTCOF-Zn and TTCOF-Cu produced 10.33 and 8.65 μmol of CO in 60 h respectively, while producing half as much O2 as CO, indicating effective CO2RR and OER coupling (Fig. 40C). The activity of TTCOF-Ni was poor due to the energy band structure mismatch and the absence of an additional sacrificial agent. Furthermore, TTCOF-Zn could be reused for at least five times without loss of its catalytic activity basically.
 Fig. 40  TTCOF-M (M = Ni, Zn, Cu) composed of metalloporphyrin and tetrathiafulvalene units for photocatalytic CO2RR and OER synchronously. (A) Schematic diagram of the TTCOF-M (M = Ni, Zn, Cu) structure. (B) Energy band structures of TTCOF-M (M = Ni, Zn, Cu). (C) The amount of CO2 and O2 catalyzed by TTCOF-M (M = Ni, Zn, Cu) under visible-light illumination after 60 h. 
Examples of metalated COF-based photocatalytic CO2RR to CO are summarized in Table 7 to provide a direct and clear understanding of the structure–function relationship of metalated COF-based photocatalysts.141,186,187,212,240,241,251,575,601–605,607–613

Metalated COFs for photocatalytic CO2RR to formic acid or formates will be discussed next. In 2019, Lan and co-workers reported a series of transition metal-loaded DQTP-COF-M (M = Co, Ni, Zn).611 As shown, the different loaded metals exert a strong influence on the activity and selectivity of products. For example, DQTP-COF-Zn has high selectivity (90% over CO) for formic acid generation with a rate of 152.5 μmol (g h)−1 under xenon lamp irradiation. In contrast, DQTP COF-Co only generated CO under the same conditions. Very recently, Trabolsi and co-workers reported a Co(II)-metalated Co-PI-COF containing isoindigo and cobalt(II) porphyrin blocks which could trigger the CO2RR in CO2-saturated acetonitrile to produce formate at an average rate of 50 μmol (g h)−1.197 Besides, Ru(0)-metalated COFs can also reduce CO2 to HCOOH.323,614 These examples imply that the products of CO2 reduction are the result of a combination of the embedded metals and COF-frameworks, which are subtle and not easily predictable in advance.
In 2020, Jiang et al. found that regulating the oxidation state of cobalt in porphyrin-COFs can regulate the generation of HCOOH.615 The Co(II) in COF-367-CoII has a low spin state (S = 1/2) with six of the seven 3d electrons occupying the t2g orbital and one occupying the eg orbital, while all six 3d electrons in COF-367-CoIII are in the t2g orbital with S = 0 (Fig. 41A). The different spin states lead to the difference in CO2RR activity because the different 3d electron distributions and orientations can change the interactions between cobalt and CO2 (Fig. 41B). Specifically, the O 2p orbitals of CO2 overlap more with Co(III) 3dz2 than with Co(II) 3dxz or 3dyz orbitals, and the interactions of HCOOH with Co(III) and Co(II) follow the same trend, both of which lead to a stronger interaction of CO2 and HCOOH with COF-367-CoIII than with COF-367-CoII. Additionally, COF-367-CoIII induces a lower energy barrier for the reduction of CO2 to HCOOH than COF-367-CoII, while the energy barrier induced by COF-367-CoIII for the further reduction of HCOOH to CO and CH4 is higher than that induced by COF-367-CoII. Therefore, compared with COF-367-CoII, COF-367-CoIII exhibits higher catalytic activity and selectivity for the photoreduction of CO2 to HCOOH. As a result, the HCOOH yield of COF-367-CoIII is 1.9 times higher than that of COF-367-CoII under the given conditions, and the HCOOH selectivity is 86% and 62%, respectively (Fig. 41C and D). This study provided a paradigm for optimizing the photocatalytic activity by tuning the electronic properties of the metal centers. In addition, more examples of CO2RR to HCOOH are listed in Table 8.197,252,323,611,614,615
 Fig. 41  Regulating the photocatalytic CO2RR to HCOOH via oxidation state manipulation of cobalt in COFs. (A) Preparation of COF-367-CoII and COF-367-CoIII with different Co spin states. (B) Different coupling modes of CO2 and HCOOH interaction with Co(II) and Co(III) sites. The numerical values represent the corresponding overlap partial density-of-state intensities based on density functional theory. (C) COF-367-CoII photocatalytic generation of HCOOH, CO, and CH4. (D) COF-367-CoIII photocatalytic generation of HCOOH, CO, and CH4. Adapted with permission of ref. 615. Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society.

3.4.3. Photocatalytic pollutant degradation. 
Although different methods have been developed for treating wastewater, there are still challenges in the environmentally sound treatment of organic pollutants in wastewater.616 Light can excite the photocatalyst to generate dissociated electrons and holes with redox activity, which can directly cause the organic pollutants’ degradation.617 In addition, photogenerated electrons and holes can also interact with water, air, or other additives (e.g., persulfate) to generate reactive oxygen species (including ˙OH, ˙O2−, 1O2, etc.), resulting in the breakage of unsaturated bonds in the pollutant molecules to degrade organic pollutants.618
In 2021, Zhao et al. examined the performance of COF-909(Cu) in the photocatalytic degradation of sulfamethoxazole.619 As shown, the absorption band of COF-909(Cu) covers almost the entire spectral range from 200 nm to 1500 nm due to the charge transfer between the COF-909 scaffold and Cu(II). In addition, COF-909(Cu) has a narrow band gap of 1.89 eV, and thus it can be more easily excited by light than COF-909 (2.34 eV). After 30 min of irradiation with a 50 W white LED, 98% of sulfamethoxazole in water was degraded. Mechanistic studies showed that photogenerated holes, ˙OH and ˙O2− are the main active substances that result in pollutant degradation. One year later, a copper-metalated Cu-Tp-Azo for the enhanced photodegradation of tetracycline was reported.620
COF-909-Fe, an analog of COF-909(Cu), is a versatile photodegradation catalyst.621 Besides a wide absorption range and a narrow band gap, COF-909-Fe has additional advantages. First, due to Fenton-like activity, COF-909-Fe can induce ˙OH production by decomposing H2O2. Second, the photogenerated holes and electrons can interact with H2O2 to generate O2 and ˙O2− upon illumination, and the diffusion of the generated active species within COF-909-Fe enhances Brownian movement and strengthens the mass transfer process. Third, the in situ O2 generated from COF-909-Fe exfoliates the large particles into nanoparticles, increasing the contact area with pollutant molecules. The obtained results showed that the particle size of COF-909-Fe was reduced from 4.3 μm to less than 400 nm under 300 mW cm−2 illumination for 1 h in 0.5% H2O2 solution, and tetracycline in water could be almost completely degraded in 2 h under the given conditions.
As we know, inorganic semiconductors, such as TiO2, Fe2O3, and CdS, have been widely used in photodegradation, but there is still room for enhancement of their performance.616 For example, the charge separation and migration rates can be further enhanced through the formation of a heterojunction composed of two inorganic semiconductors.622 However, this strategy does not solve the problems of their narrow light absorption range and poor long-term stability. The application of heterojunctions formed by inorganic semiconductors and organic materials (such as COFs) in photodegradation has been noticed.618 The π-conjugated COFs with a wide light absorption range, good chemical stability and a protective effect on inorganic semiconductors would lead to durable and efficient heterojunctions.623
Bismuth oxyhalides represent a class of semiconductor materials with a layered skeleton. It has been proven that these materials possess certain photocatalytic activities. Recently, Bachas et al. assembled azine-linked ACOF-1 with BiOBr to form a type II heterojunction ACOF-1@BiOBr via a hydrothermal reaction of bismuth nitrate and sodium bromide in the presence of ACOF-1.624 The complete degradation of methyl orange, rhodamine B, and methylene blue by ACOF-1@BiOBr only required 140, 40 and 40 min, respectively, under a 300 W mercury-xenon lamp, which was significantly shorter than when using ACOF-1 and BiOBr alone. The photogenerated electrons were enriched in the conduction band of BiOBr, and the photogenerated holes were enriched in the valence-band of ACOF-1 upon irradiation, which optimized the separation and migration of photogenerated carriers and enhanced the photocatalytic properties. The photogenerated holes and electron-induced ˙O2− are the main active species for dye degradation. Based on the same strategy, BiOBr and TzDa COF were assembled to form a Z-scheme heterojunction BiOBr/TzDa, which can degrade rhodamine B by a variety of active substances including photogenerated holes, ˙O2− and ˙OH.625 In addition, the photogenerated electrons participated in the reduction of Cr(VI) to treat wastewater. Furthermore, Z-scheme heterojunctions based on Ag(I) semiconductors, including AgI/COF-PD626 and Ag3PO4/TpPa-1,377 have also been reported. They all show a good performance for the degradation of rhodamine B, acetaminophen, and pymetrozine. These studies emphasized that the different pollutant degradation paths can be realized by adjusting the COF-structures to meet the requirements for wastewater treatment.
The photoactivity of inorganic semiconductors is highly dependent on the particle size and specific surface area since the photodegradation reaction occurs at the particle surface. Reducing the particle size to greater than 10 nm can greatly improve their photocatalytic activity, but this method is often limited by nanoparticle agglomeration and deactivation. Embedding ultrasmall nanoparticles into COFs can ameliorate these deficiencies. For example, perovskites have very poor water stability and cannot be directly used for photodegradation. However, the EB-COF:Br-supported perovskite displays good cycling stability and degradation efficiency in the photocatalytic degradation of methyl orange in water.373 In addition, the role of COF-supports in the protection of ultrasmall gold nanoparticles has been validated, including but not limited to photocatalytic degradation applications.334,627 Another interesting example is TpTa-COF-supported ultrasmall Fe(III)-doped TiO2 nanoparticles (called Fe–TiO2) by a COF-supported confined growth method, which was reported by Zhang et al.369 The authors suggested that the interactions between amine and carbonyl groups of the COF with TiO2 provide favourable anchoring sites, facilitating the nucleation and growth of nanoparticles inside the COF pores. Upon 365 nm LED irradiation, methylene blue was completely degraded by Fe–TiO2@TpTa-COF within 20 min, while a 98% degradation rate was achieved within 180 min upon ambient light irradiation.
Due to the complex composition of sewage, photocatalysts that are designed to simultaneously degrade different types of pollutants, including organics, microplastics, heavy metal ions, and radionuclides, are urgently needed.628–634 The secondary pollution caused by photocatalysts is also a potential issue that requires attention. Due to the good chemical and photostability of COFs, the synthesis of multifunctional photodegradation catalysts based on multifunctional metalated COFs may be a promising research direction.


3.4.4. Photocatalytic organic transformations. 
In the photocatalysis mentioned above, photocatalytic energy is mainly achieved through photoinduced separation and migration of electrons and holes; therefore, the match between the energy level of the photocatalyst and the redox potential of half-reaction is very important.635 This match can be optimized by adjusting the metal species in COFs. However, in some cases, coulombic interactions between electrons and holes may be significantly promoted in the 2D COFs, leading to different photoexcitation mechanisms.636 Electrons and holes in bound states are called excitons, and their triplet states are crucial for promoting photocatalytic reactions.637 The spin–orbit coupling of metal-free semiconductors is weak, and the intersystem crossing rate is usually slow, leading the excitons to exist primarily in a singlet state. Heavy-atom doping (e.g., metal and iodine atoms) can be used to optimize the generation of triplet excitons by enhancing intersystem crossing. The interaction of the triplet excitons with the substrate leads to abundant reaction chemistry, the most common of which is the energy exchange between triplet excitons and ground oxygen through the Dexter coupling mechanism, resulting in the formation of 1O2, an excellent oxidant for green chemical synthesis.638 In this section, we will introduce the application of photocatalysis in organic transformation reactions involving the two aforementioned mechanisms, excitonic and charge-carrier aspects.
In 2020, Jiang et al. reported an example of changing the photosensitization mechanism by adjusting central metal ions in metalloporphyrin-COFs.144 As indicated in Fig. 42, the zinc porphyrin-based DhaTph-Zn COF converted singlet excitons into triplet excitons by facilitating energy transfer, thus inducing 1O2 generation. In contrast, the nickel porphyrin-based DhaTph-Ni COF promoted photoinduced electron transfer by inducing the dissociation of excitons into holes and electrons, leading to ˙O2− production. The two different active species lead to their different selectivity in α-terpinene oxidation. Upon irradiation with visible light from a 300 W xenon lamp, the DhaTph-Zn COF led to the oxidation of α-terpinene by 1O2 to 1-isopropyl-4-methyl-2,3-dioxabicyclo[2.2.2]oct-5-ene with 99% conversion and 85% selectivity, while the DhaTph-Ni COF exhibited 76% conversion and 81% selectivity toward p-cymene due to ˙O2− generation under the same conditions. Furthermore, the two COFs are suitable for different photocatalytic reactions. For example, the DhaTph-Ni COF can effectively catalyse the oxidative hydroxylation of phenylboronic acid to phenol (99% yield), while the DhaTph-Zn COF displays a high catalytic activity for the oxidation of methyl(phenyl)sulfane to (methylsulfinyl)benzene (82% yield). This interesting study shows that the rational design of photosensitive COFs with different metal species can facilitate the efficient synthesis of specific products by regulating excitonic effects.
 Fig. 42  Tuning of photocatalytic selectivity by changing metals in metalloporphyrin-based COFs. (A) Structures of DhaTph-Zn and DhaTph-Ni and their different photosensitizing activities. (B) Yields of three kinds of photocatalytic reactions over DhaTph-Zn and DhaTph-Ni photocatalysts under an O2 atmosphere (1 atm) in acetonitrile upon visible-light irradiation. 
In 2020, Cui et al. reported a copper(II) porphyrin-based CuCOF2 with 3D tho topology, by which the generated 1O2 and ˙O2− can promote the conversion of phenylboronic acids to the corresponding phenol derivatives under 440 nm LED irradiation (81–93% yields).101 With cysteine and sodium formate, CuCOF2 can also catalyse the defluoroalkylation of trifluoromethyl-substituted aromatics and olefins in moderate-to-good yields but with a free radical mechanism induced by photogenerated carrier separation.
Visible-light photoredox catalysis is an invaluable tool in modern organic synthesis, in which light active metal-based catalysts (e.g., Ru-, Ir-, and Au-based complexes) with long excited-state lifetimes and high redox potentials allow the formation of radical intermediates, enabling substrate activation and driving the reaction.639–641 Modification of photoredox catalysts on COF-scaffolds makes the visible-light heterogeneous catalysis available. For example, the Neogi group reported that the tris(2,2′-bipyridine)ruthenium(II)-derived Ru-TAPM-BPDCA-COF is a useful photoredox catalyst for the Strecker reaction (Fig. 43A).254 In water, N,N-dimethylaniline was converted to 2-(methyl(phenyl)amino)acetonitrile with a yield of 99%. In this process, the light induced exciton separation; the photogenerated electrons induced ˙O2− generation via single electron transfer; and the photogenerated holes oxidized N,N-dimethylaniline to the radical cation, completing the activation of the substrates. Furthermore, Neogi et al. reported similar Ru(II)-based COF photocatalysts for visible-light-mediated photoredox cross-dehydrogenative coupling of glycine esters with indoles, obtaining 19 coupling products in 45–95% yields (Fig. 43B).247
 Fig. 43  Ru-, Ni-, and Pt-Metalated COFs for visible-light photoredox catalysis. (A) Strecker reaction of N,N-dimethylaniline and trimethylsilyl cyanide. (B) Dehydrogenative coupling of glycine esters and indoles. (C) Photocatalytic S–C cross-coupling reaction between aryl iodides and thiols. (D) Decarboxylative difluoroalkylation and oxidative cyclization. 
In 2021, Van Der Voort et al. reported a triazine-based imine-linked COF decorated with single Ni sites (Fig. 43C).290 The obtained Ace-COF-Ni is an active photoredox catalyst for catalyzing the cross-coupling reaction between aryl iodides and thiols under visible light. This is the first report of integration of single transition metal active sites in a photosensitive COF with a two-component synergetic catalyst for organic transformation.
By post-synthetic metalation of an imine-linked COF with Pt(II) occurring selectively at the N donor (imine and pyridyl) in the larger pores, Beyzavi and co-workers synthesized Pt(II)-complex-loaded COF-UARK-49-Pt (Fig. 43D).288 The generated COF-UARK-49-Pt possesses an absorption band covering almost the entire visible spectrum and a band gap of 2.0 eV and can be a recyclable heterogeneous photocatalyst for decarboxylative difluoroalkylation and oxidative cyclization reactions.
Additionally, other metalated COFs have also been reported for photocatalysis, including Fe(III)-,226 Ti(IV)-,96 Bi(III)-,642 Pt(II)-,643 and Pd(II)-metalated COFs,305 all of which achieve substrate activation via photoinduced exciton dissociation.
In addition to homometallic COFs, heterobimetallic COFs have also been reported for photocatalysis. In 2021, Mas-Ballesté and co-workers reported Ir,Ni@Phen-COF which can promote the light-mediated Csp3−Csp2 cross-coupling between potassium benzyltrifluoroborates and aryl bromides.261 Upon illumination by a blue LED, the excited state Ir(III) activates the substrate through single electron transfer, while Ni(II) is responsible for the following coupling between the activated substrate and aryl bromides. Later on, Maji et al. reported a Ni-Ir@TpBpy COF which was prepared by integrating photoredox-active Ir(III) and Ni(II) in the 2,2′-bipyridine-involving TpBpy COF by dual post-synthetic metalation for performing a variety of photocatalytic C–N cross-coupling reactions.242 Of special note, the utility of the Ni-Ir@TpBpy COF is also highlighted by the preparation of a variety of drug molecules including flufenamic acid, tripelennamine, and flibanserin. In these two interesting cases, the cooperativity between Ir(III) and Ni(II) allows the heterobimetallic COF to display higher catalytic activity and stability than its homogeneous counterpart.
Due to the large π-conjugated structure, metalated COFs in fact have emerged as ideal platforms for integrating light capture and photocatalytic sites with all the advantages of heterostructured photocatalysts, including recyclability, chemical stability, and photostability. However, one of the major drawbacks of the currently reported metalated COF photocatalysts is their excessive dependency on the high-energy visible light (e.g., blue light). Many photosensitizers (e.g., metalloporphyrins, metallophthalocyanines, and Ru-based N3 dye) have broad absorption in the green, red, or even near-infrared range. Their redox potentials and excited state energies are theoretically suitable for photocatalysis, but the low intersystem crossing efficiency of these chromophores leads to poor catalytic performance, requiring further optimization. Using natural sunlight rather than artificial light sources for sustainable photochemical reactions may be a future research direction, which underscores the urgency of research on solar energy capture and photocatalytic strategies covering the entire visible spectrum.644


3.4.5. Photothermal catalysis. 
The coupling of light energy and thermal energy is a promising strategy to drive chemical reactions, which is known as photothermal catalysis.645,646 According to the reaction mechanism, photothermal catalysis can be divided into three subtypes.647
Type I: thermal-assisted photocatalysis. The high temperature (which is usually achieved by an external heat source) promotes photogenerated carrier migration and accelerates the photochemical reaction. Therefore, the primary reaction mechanism is still a photochemical pathway. This subtype has been widely used in the conversion chemistry of CO2, including methanation, reverse water gas shift reactions, and dry reforming reactions.647,648
Type II: photo-assisted thermocatalysis. In this subtype, the photothermal material absorbs light energy and converts it to thermal energy via localized surface plasmon resonance heating or nonradiative relaxation.648,649 Local overheating replaces the external heat source. The reaction occurring at elevated temperature still proceeds in the same way as the traditional thermally driven process, in which photogenerated charge carriers or excitons do not contribute to reactant activation in this subtype.650 To achieve the desired reaction temperature, the ability to absorb light and the photothermal conversion efficiency of materials are crucial. Materials with good photothermal properties include gold nanorods, nonstoichiometric metal chalcogenides, carbon materials, etc.651–653
Type III: photothermal cocatalysis. The photoreaction mechanism and thermal reaction mechanism intertwine or form a cascade of steps to drive the reaction, leading to a synergistic effect of photochemical and thermochemical pathways, as opposed to a simple addition of the two pathways.648
Copper porphyrins are common organic photothermal conversion materials due to the enhanced nonradiative transitions by Cu(II). Dong and co-workers successively reported a series of copper porphyrin-based CCOFs for type II photothermal catalysis (Fig. 44).143,145,654
 Fig. 44  Copper porphyrin-based COFs for photothermal asymmetric catalysis. (A) Au- and Pd-Loaded CCOF-CuTPP for catalyzing asymmetric coupling reactions. (B) Homochiral (R)-CuTAPBN-COF for catalyzing the enantioselective Strecker reaction. (C) (R)-CuTAPBP-COF as a chiral catalyst for the α-benzylation of aldehydes. 
In 2019, gold nanorods and palladium nanoparticles were introduced into CCOF-CuTPP as coupling reaction catalysts by in situ reduction, yielding Au@CCOF-CuTPP and Pd@CCOF-CuTPP respectively (Fig. 44A).143 Under visible-light irradiation, Au@CCOF-CuTPP induced the temperature of the reaction system (toluene/ethanol) to increase from room temperature to 58 °C, which greatly promoted the Henry reaction of benzyl alcohols with nitromethane in 93–99% yields with 94–98% ee. Meanwhile, Pd@CCOF-CuTPP induced the temperature to rise to 45 °C in dioxane and efficiently catalysed the three-component coupling reaction of aromatic aldehydes, aryl acetylenes, and cyclic secondary amines in 68–98% yields with ee above 90%. In addition, the chiral CCOF-CuTPP support provides a single chiral restriction space for high enantioselectivity.
In 2020, (R)-CuTAPBN-COF was synthesized by integrating chiral binaphthalene and copper(II) porphyrin into a single COF-framework (Fig. 44B).145 Of note, the catalytic Cu(II) sites and the chirality control of chiral binaphthalene species are not on the same entity, resembling what is happening in an enzyme pocket. Upon light irradiation (xenon lamp, 2.5 W cm−2), the reaction system (acetonitrile) increased from room temperature to 50 °C within 18 min, which significantly promoted the Cu(II)-catalysed Strecker reaction of 2-chlorobenzaldehyde, 4,5,6,7-tetrahydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridine and trimethylsilyl cyanide to obtain (S)-2-(2-chlorophenyl)-2-(6,7-dihydrothieno[3,2-c]pyridin-5(4H)-yl)acetonitrile—a key intermediate for the synthesis of the antithrombotic drug (S)-clopidogrel—in 98% yield with 94% ee. Interestingly, the (R)-CuTAPBN-COF-induced Strecker reaction could also be effectively driven by natural sunlight, with a slightly decreased yield of 70% but with 92% ee. This result not only establishes a new synthetic process for the synthesis of (S)-clopidogrel but also affords a foundation for development of COF-based and solar-powered endothermic asymmetric catalysis.
Later on, Dong group reported additional Cu(II)-metalated CCOF by a similar approach (Fig. 44C).654 The obtained (R)-CuTAPBP-COF contains both Brønsted acid (phosphoric acid) and Lewis acid (copper porphyrin) catalytic sites. Upon visible-light irradiation, it can highly promote the intermolecular asymmetric α-benzylation of aldehydes via visible-light-induced photothermal conversion: for example, the reaction of propionaldehyde and 4-(bromomethyl)pyridine in methanol to form (R)-2-methyl-3-(pyridin-4-yl)propanal in 98% yield with 95% ee. Again, this type II photothermal catalytic reaction can effectively proceed under natural sunlight irradiation.
In addition to copper(II) porphyrin-COFs, the application of cobalt(II) porphyrin-COFs in photothermal catalytic CO2 cycloaddition was recently reported by Dong et al.655 In COF-PI-2, the cobalt(II) porphyrin and benzoimidazolium served as a photothermal conversion site and a catalytic site, respectively. Under irradiation with a xenon lamp at 80 mW cm−2, COF-PI-2 induced an increase in the temperature of the reaction system from 25 °C to 51 °C, which promoted the reaction of epibromohydrin with 1 atm CO2 to obtain 4-(bromomethyl)-1,3-dioxolan-2-one in basically quantitative yield. Interestingly, the CO2 cycloaddition herein is a “window ledge” reaction, and it can be conducted upon natural sunlight irradiation with a cycloaddition yield of up to 90%.

3.5. Thermocatalysis
Since the catalytic application of metalated COFs was first reported in 2011,129 the studies on metalated COF catalysts have drawn more and more attention.112–115,656–658 Metalated COFs have some unique advantages over conventional metal-based catalysts. First, COFs contain abundant and mutually independent 1D pore channels, which effectively enhance mass transfer and facilitate the contact of reactants with catalytic active sites, theoretically making all metal sites accessible.659 Second, the metal sites are uniformly distributed in the COF-matrix, preventing the leakage, agglomeration, and deactivation of the metal catalyst during the reaction.660–662 Third, COFs are highly stable, and thus, their structural integrity usually remains intact after multiple uses, which facilitates the recycling and reuse of catalysts and reduces the consumption of precious metals.112 Furthermore, the flexibility and versatility of COF design allow realization of multifunctional catalytic systems, such as chemo- and regioselectivity, asymmetric catalysis, and stereospecific polymerization.663,664
This section will be divided into 16 subsections according to the types of metal elements (Fig. 45), and each subsection will discuss the heterogeneous catalytic applications of metalated COFs along the order from high to low oxidation state.
 Fig. 45  Metal elements and their oxidation numbers in metalated COF catalysts. 

3.5.1. Palladium-metalated COF catalysts. 
Palladium catalysts, including Pd(II) and Pd(0), are commonly used catalysts with high catalytic activity, high selectivity and low usage.665,666 The alumina, zeolite, and carbon carrier supported Pd-catalysts can be repeatedly regenerated and activated; thus, they have a long service life.667,668 As a promising class of candidates for catalyst carriers, COFs have shown a unique value in palladium-catalysed reactions, including Suzuki–Miyaura, Heck, Sonogashira coupling reactions, and many others, making many difficult-to-achieve reaction processes possible.113,663
Pd(II)-Coordinated COF-LZU1, namely Pd/COF-LZU1, which was reported by Wang et al. in 2011, is the first example of metalated COFs used for heterogeneous catalysis.129 The Suzuki–Miyaura coupling reaction of aryl iodides with phenylboronic acid at 150 °C in p-xylene catalyzed by 0.5 mol% Pd/COF-LZU1 afforded the corresponding coupling products in yields of 96–97% (Fig. 46A). The less reactive bromobenzene derivatives also resulted in high yields of over 95% in 2.5–4 h. Furthermore, in the reaction of 1-bromo-4-nitrobenzene and phenylboronic acid, Pd/COF-LZU1 was reused at least four times without significant loss of activity but along with a slight crystallinity decrease.
 Fig. 46  COF-supported Pd(II) for heterogeneous catalysis. (A) Suzuki–Miyaura coupling reactions of phenylboronic acid and aromatic halogens. (B) Region-selective Suzuki–Miyaura cross-coupling reaction of phenylboronic acid and aromatic halogens. (C) Heck reaction of styrene and iodobenzene. (D) Heck reaction of an olefin and phenylborate ester. (E) Conjugate addition of cinnamic acid and phenylboronic acid. (F) Region-selective alkoxylation of benzo[h]quinoline. (G) C–P coupling reaction of an aryl halide and secondary phosphine oxide. (H) Intramolecular oxidative coupling of diphenylamine. 
The lack of selectivity of Pd-catalysts in Suzuki–Miyaura coupling reactions makes it difficult to efficiently obtain halogen-substituted biphenyl-type compounds. In 2021, Chen et al. reported a series of COF-supported Pd-catalysts, namely Pd/COF-SMCx (X = 1, 2, 3, 4).669 The authors demonstrated that they can regioselectively catalyse the Suzuki–Miyaura cross-coupling reaction of arylboronic acids and aryl halides, especially with halogenated arylboronic acids (Fig. 46B).
Triarylbismuth compounds are an eco-friendly alternative to phenylboronic acids. In 2019, Wang et al. reported that the coupling of 1-iodo-4-nitrobenzene with triphenylbismuthane afforded the coupling product in 96% yield in the presence of COF-NHC–Pd and K2CO3 at room temperature.278 Encouragingly, COF-NHC–Pd had satisfactory catalytic activity for the substrates containing either electron-withdrawing or electron-donating groups.
The Heck reaction is another famous Pd-catalysed coupling reaction between unsaturated halohydrocarbons and alkenes for the construction of C–C bonds.670 In 2016, Chai et al. reported the Pd(II)@X% BPy COF with different Pd loading for the Heck reaction (Fig. 46C).671 The authors demonstrated that the Pd(II)@75% BPy COF afforded the best catalytic performance. For example, the coupling of iodobenzene and styrene afforded stilbene with a yield greater than 90% in DMF with K2CO3.
A key challenge for the Heck reaction is the regioselectivity (limiting the insertion sites of the halogenated hydrocarbons) and stereochemical selectivity (obtaining an alkene with a single configuration). Recently, Ji et al. synthesized a Pd/COF-BTDH catalyst, in which Pd(II) coordinated to the imine linkage and its neighbouring hydroxyl group.292 With the help of Pd/COF-BTDH and copper(II) trifluoromethanesulfonate, the coupling of oct-1-ene with 2-phenyl-1,3,2-dioxaborolane afforded (E)-oct-1-en-1-ylbenzene in 74% yield in the oxygenated N,N-dimethylacetamide at 40 °C for 48 h (Fig. 46D). The ratio of (E)-oct-1-en-1-ylbenzene and oct-1-en-2-ylbenzene was up to 50:1, and the ratio of (E)-oct-1-en-1-ylbenzene with other isomers reached 7:1. However, these two ratios were reduced to 5:1 and 1.3:1 when no COF-BTDH was present. Notably, the attempt to replace Pd/COF-BTDH with Pd/COF-LZU1 gave a disappointing result, suggesting that the pore wall environment of COF-BTDH played an important role in regulating the selectivity for the Heck reaction. Of course, the Pd/COF-BTDH-catalysed Heck reaction could be generally applied to a variety of aliphatic olefins, including styrene, vinylcyclohexane, and 4,8-dimethylnona-1,7-diene.
Pd(II) bound to 2,2′-bipyridine-based COFs with a wide range of catalytic activities has been used as a recyclable catalyst for various organic transformations. In 2021, Pan et al. reported a Pd(II)@TpBpy-COF which was demonstrated to be a catalyst for conjugate addition of arylboronic acids to unreactive α,β-unsaturated carboxylic acids in aqueous media (water/acetone) (Fig. 46E).225 The yield was maintained at more than 70% after five catalytic cycles. The same year, Ma et al. reported an interesting Pd(II)-loaded COF catalyst based on an imine-linked dual-pore COF which can catalyse regioselective hydrocarbon activation reaction of benzo[h]quinoline in a heterogeneous way (Fig. 46F).243 Of note, the authors compared the catalytic activity of the Pd-loaded imine-linked dual-pore COF with its single-pore counterpart. The dual-pore Pd(II)@[(BPyDC)]x%-ETTA was more active than the single-pore Pd(II)@Py-2,2′-BPyDC because the dual-pore COF might be more favorable for the reactant and product diffusion within the framework, as suggested by the authors.
One year later, Zhang et al. reported a Pd(II)-coordinated azine-linked TFPT-Azine-COF for dehalogenative phosphorylation to form C–P bonds.672 It was found that the synthesized Pd/TFPT-Azine-COF is an efficient heterogeneous catalyst for the Hirao reaction of bromobenzene with diphenylphosphine oxide to form triphenylphosphine oxide with 94% yield (Fig. 46G). In addition, Pd/TFPT-Azine-COF was suitable for a variety of para-substituted substrates, including fluoro-, chloro-, alkoxy-, nitro-, and formyl-substituted aryl halides. More importantly, Pd/TFPT-Azine-COF was stable under the given reaction conditions with no Pd(II) leaching or loss of activity after seven successive catalytic cycles.
Furthermore, the Pd(II)-anchored TpPa-1 COF was shown to be active for the intramolecular oxidative biaryl coupling to convert diphenylamine to 9H-carbazole with a 68% yield in pivalic acid (Fig. 46H).673
Compared with Pd(II), Pd(0) is more commonly used in heterogeneous catalysis for various organic functional group transformations (Fig. 47) and C–C coupling reactions (Fig. 48). Pd(0) embedded in a COF-matrix circumvents the aggregation of Pd nanoparticles during the catalytic process. For example, in 2020, Yue et al. reported Pd@COF-300 in which the amino defects in COF-300 were essential for obtaining ultrasmall monodisperse Pd nanoparticles.332 With increasing amino content, the Pd particle size decreased from above 5.0 nm to approximately 1.6 nm. As expected, Pd nanoparticles with a size of 1.6 nm showed the best conversion and selectivity for the oxidation of phenylmethanol to benzaldehyde. Because the framework with amino defects significantly stabilized the Pd nanoparticles, the catalyst was reused six times without any significant loss of performance.
 Fig. 47  COF-supported Pd(0) as a heterogeneous catalyst of functional group transformations. 
 Fig. 48  Pd(0) nanoparticles loaded into COFs for heterogeneous catalysis of C–C bond formation reactions. (A) Pd@TpBpy COF as a heterogeneous catalyst for catalyzing the reaction of 2-bromophenol derivatives and terminal alkynes. (B) Pd@CCOF-MPC for catalyzing asymmetric Henry reactions of nitromethane and aromatic aldehydes. (C) Pd@CCOF-MPC for catalyzing asymmetric reductive Heck reactions of cyclohex-2-en-1-one and aryl bromides. 
It is known that the COF-supported Pd nanoparticles can act as a powerful catalyst for a series of organic reduction reactions, including nitro group reduction,14,316,328 partial hydrogenation of the alkyne group,317 and dehalogenation of chlorobenzenes.311,315 In 2018, Dong et al. reported COF-AO-supported Pd nanoparticles (termed as Pd@COF-AO) which displayed excellent catalytic performance in the conversion of 4-chlorophenol to phenol in ammonium acetate aqueous solution.311 After the catalyst was repeatedly used 5 times, no detectable aggregation or Pd leaching was observed, and its catalytic activity remained almost unchanged. The incorporation of Pd@COF-AO with thiol-functionalized polysiloxane via thiol−ene click reaction provided the standalone and elastic membrane. The obtained membrane was robust, permanently porous, uniform, processable, and water-permeable. Moreover, it can be used to construct highly efficient membrane-based microreactors for continuous-flow operation to promote chlorobenzene dechlorination in water at room temperature.
The same year, the same group fabricated Pd@COF@chitosan aerogels by the cross-linking of Pd@TpTe-1@chitosan with 1,4-butanediol diglycidyl ether by a templated freeze-drying procedure. The resulting Pd@COF@chitosan aerogels can be used to build highly efficient continuous flow-through microreactors for chlorobenzene dechlorination in water at room temperature.315 The incorporation of COFs with polymer binders allows the processability of metalated COFs to be feasible, accelerating the construction of metalated COF-based devices for various applications.
In addition to reduction reactions, Pd(0) was also applied in C–C bond formation.271,312,313 In 2021, Zhao et al. reported SP-3D-COF-BPY-supported Pd nanoparticles for Suzuki–Miyaura coupling reaction.248 At the same Pd content, PdNP@SP-3D-COF-BPY showed less activity than Pd(II)@SP-3D-COF-BPY, which may result from the regular arrangement of Pd(II) in the COF-scaffold that can minimize the aggregation of the key intermediate Pd(0) in the catalytic process.
The application of Pd(0)-loaded COFs for catalytic synthesis of 2-substituted benzofurans was reported by the Banerjee group (Fig. 48A).224 Pd(0) nanoparticles with a size of 12 ± 4 nm were uniformly dispersed in the TpBpy COF at a content of 15.2 wt%. In the presence of the Pd@TpBpy COF and K2CO3, the reaction of 2-bromophenol and ethynylbenzene in DMF gave 2-phenylbenzofuran in 70% yield, while the commercially available Pd/C catalyst provided only 23% yield under the same conditions. In addition, this benzofuran synthesis was compatible with a variety of groups, including –CN, –NO2, –CHO, and halogen located on 2-bromophenol; alkyl, alkoxyl, halogen, –COCH3, and –N(CH3)2 located on phenylethynes; and aliphatic alkynes (e.g., ethynyltriisopropylsilane and ethynylcyclopropane), to afford the corresponding benzofuran products in 26–80% yields.
The CCOF-supported Pd nanoparticles for asymmetric synthesis were achieved in 2017.140 Dong and co-workers synthesized the C–N single bond-linked CCOF-MPC by heating a mixture of 2,4,6-trichloro-1,3,5-triazine and (S)-2-methylpiperazine in a dioxane solution of K2CO3 at 90 °C. After solution impregnation with Pd(NO3)2 in methanol and subsequent NaBH4 reduction at room temperature, Pd@CCOF-MPC was obtained as a brown-black powder with a palladium content up to 34.1 wt%. The incorporation of Pd(0) and the CCOF-framework allowed Pd@CCOF-MPC to be a highly active reusable asymmetric heterogeneous composite catalyst to effectively promote the Henry reaction (Fig. 48B) and reductive Heck reaction (Fig. 48C) in high yield with excellent stereoselectivity. For large-sized substrates, both Henry and reductive Heck reactions, however, afforded the desired products in quite low yields without any enantioselectivity, demonstrating that the reactions are intrapore catalytic processes.
In addition, COF-supported Pd nanoparticles have also been employed to catalyse tandem reactions. In 2014, Banerjee and co-workers realized the Pd(0)@TpPa-1-catalyzed sequential Heck–Sonogashira reactions under basic conditions. The catalytic activity was superior to that of the commercially available Pd/C catalyst.673 These Heck–Sonogashira cross-coupling reactions provided an effective method for preparing (alkynyl)(alkenyl)arenes in good yields, highlighting the usability of the catalyst for the regioselective formation of two C−C bonds in a one pot reaction.


3.5.2. Iridium-metalated COF catalysts. 
It is well-known that iridium-based homogeneous catalysts are widely used in catalytic hydrogenation, C–H activation, and C–C coupling reactions.674 However, the reports of iridium-metalated COF catalysts are relatively rare, although they are logically feasible.
As reported by the Beyzavi group, Ir(III) can be coordinated into the imine-linked COF by forming N–Ir and C–Ir bonds.289 The obtained iridacycle-decorated Py-1P-Ir COF exhibited more than tenfold efficiency enhancement in catalytic H2 evolution from sodium formate aqueous solution than its homogeneous counterpart. Of note, Py-1P-Ir COF could be reused at least five times without any significant decrease in crystallinity or catalytic performance, although partial imine bonds were reduced to C–N bonds.
Bipyridine-COF can also be employed to bind iridium for organic catalysis. In 2019, Ma and co-workers immobilized Ir(I) on a 2D Py-2,2′-BPyPh COF (Fig. 49).245 The obtained Ircod(I)@Py-2,2′-BPyPh exhibited excellent catalytic activity for C−H borylation of aromatic rings. Under the same conditions, neither the [Ir(μ-OCH3)(1,5-cyclooctadiene)]2 complex nor Py-2,2′-BPyPh COF exhibits catalytic activity. Furthermore, Ircod(I)@Py-2,2′-BPyPh possessed satisfactory recyclability and was compatible with a variety of electronically and sterically substituted substrates.
 Fig. 49  Iridium(I)-immobilized Ircod(I)@Py-2,2′-BPyPh COF for C–H borylation. 


3.5.3. Rhodium-metalated COF catalysts. 
Precise selectivity control in the metal-catalysed olefin hydroformylation is a long-standing challenge.675 In 2020, Fan et al. reported the triphenylphosphine-based COFs loaded with (acetylacetonato)dicarbonylrhodium(I) via Rh–P coordination bonds.273 The COF-confined Rh catalysts, Rh–P-COF, showed high TOF (above 2000 h−1), high aldehyde selectivity (99%) and excellent catalyst reusability (up to 5 cycles) for hydroformylation of olefins. The common side reactions, such as hydrogenation to alkane and isomerization to 2-alkene, were negligible. However, the regioselectivity in hydroformylation was dissatisfactory. For example, the hydroformylation of styrene yielded linear and branched aldehydes with a molar ratio of 1.1:1. Similar regioselectivity was also detected in the reactions involving straight-chain α-olefins, such as hex-1-ene and oct-1-ene. No regioselectivity was observed in the 4-chlorostyrene hydroformylation, yielding equal amounts of 3-(4-chlorophenyl)propanal and 2-(4-chlorophenyl)propanal.
In addition, the Rh-complexes can also be loaded by the COFs via metal–linkage coordination. By post-treatment of the chloro(2,5-norbornadiene)rhodium(I) dimer with TPB-DMTP-COF via ligand exchange, Li et al. prepared TPB-DMTP-COF-Rh, in which chloro(2,5-norbornadiene)rhodium(I) was stabilized by hydroxyl O and imine N atoms.676 TPB-DMTP-COF-Rh with 2.74 wt% Rh can be a highly active catalyst to promote the polymerization of phenylethyne to cis-transoidal polyphenylacetylene in toluene with high activity and selectivity. The polymerization catalyzed by TPB-DMTP-COF-Rh therein resulted in a similar or higher molecular weight and narrower molecular weight distribution than the polymerization catalyzed by the corresponding homogeneous catalysis due to the steric confinement of COF-pores. These catalytic reactions are also suitable for large alkynes. For example, the polymerization of alkynes with tetraphenylethene resulted in polymers with aggregation-induced emission properties, while the polymerization of an alkyne with an N,N-dimethylnaphthalen-1-amine block resulted in a pH-responsive fluorescent polymer. Due to the hydrophobicity of TPB-DMTP-COF-Rh, phenylacetylene easily enters the pores, and thus, the polymerization can be carried out in air and water. Of note, the catalytic activity of TPB-DMTP-COF-Rh significantly decreased after three catalytic cycles because the COF-pores were blocked by the generated polymers. This study highlights the promising application of metalated COFs in improving the stereoselectivity of polymerization.
In addition, COF-supported Rh(0) catalysts have also been reported. The Rh nanoparticle-loaded Rh/COF2 with 3.3 wt% Rh is capable of converting benzaldehyde to N-benzyl-1-phenylmethanimine via a one-pot tandem reductive amination in ammonia methanol solution at 2 MPa H2 with greater than 99% conversion and greater than 95% selectivity, as reported by Ma and co-workers in 2021.350


3.5.4. Ruthenium-metalated COF catalysts. 
One of the challenges of olefin metathesis is the competition between intramolecular ring-closing metathesis and intermolecular oligomerization. Very recently, Lotsch et al. reported a Ru@dHP-TAP COF catalyst with a Ru content of 42.3 μmol g−1 by loading a Hoveyda–Grubbs-type NHC–ruthenium species into the dHP-TAP COF.279 The authors demonstrated that the Ru@dHP-TAP COF catalyst exhibited an interesting size-dependent olefin metathesis driven by the spatial confinement of dHP-TAP-COF. For dec-9-en-1-yl undec-10-enoate which can enter the COF-pores, the reaction exhibited higher selectivity for intramolecular ring-closing metathesis to afford oxacycloicos-11-en-2-one. In contrast, the oversized dodecane-1,12-diyl bis(undec-10-enoate) mainly underwent the intermolecular reaction on the catalyst surface to give the oligomerization product.
Besides Ru ions, Ru(0) nanoparticles can also be supported and stabilized by COF-frameworks. In 2018, Dong and co-workers reported that heteroatom-enriched COF-ASB was an ideal support to load Ru nanoparticles to form Ru@COF-ASB. As shown, the obtained Ru@COF-ASB is able to highly promote one-pot tandem synthesis of imine products from benzyl alcohols and the corresponding amines under solvent-free conditions in air.321
Later on, a RuO2@TpBD-Me2 catalyst loaded with nanoscale RuO2 was reported by Kolen’ko and co-workers. It was demonstrated to be a good catalyst for formic acid dehydrogenation, as it showed higher catalytic activity than the commercial Ru/C catalyst.367


3.5.5. Platinum-metalated COF catalysts. 
Due to their high surface area and surface-area-to-volume ratio, ultrasmall-sized Pt nanoparticles are an efficient heterogeneous catalyst and have attracted particular interest. In 2017, Zhang et al. prepared Pt nanoparticles with a size of 1.7 nm within the Thio-COF pores using a wet chemical method.312 The presence of thioether groups inside the ordered COF pores plays a central role in the size-controlled growth of ultrafine Pt nanoparticles. The Pt@Thio-COF showed excellent catalytic activity in nitrophenol reduction, and could completely convert 4-nitrophenol to 4-aminophenol within 8 min. Notably, Pt@Thio-COF was very stable and could be readily recovered by natural sedimentation and it showed almost constant performance over six catalytic cycles. Later on, the Pt-metalated COF catalyst PtNPs@Phos-COF-1, which possessed similar catalytic activity for reduction of 4-nitrophenol, was also reported by Zhang et al.271
Also, COF-supported Pt(0) can catalyse reductive amination with hydrogen and ammonia to prepare symmetrical secondary amines.354 The Pt/COF catalyst was prepared by Ma et al. via loading Pt nanoparticles (approximately 2 nm) into a COF generated from 1,2,4,5-tetrakis(4-formylphenyl)benzene and 4,4′-oxydianiline. As shown in Fig. 50, Pt/COF specifically converted benzaldehyde to dibenzylamine in 96% yield under the given reaction conditions, which is largely superior to the performance of commercial Pt/C. In addition, the commercial Pt/C, Rh/C, and Ru/C catalysts, however, led to additional products including imine, benzylamine and benzyl alcohol, while RANEY® Ni and Co catalysts resulted in the formation of benzylamine and a secondary amine under the same conditions. Without catalysts, the reaction mainly afforded N,N′-(phenylmethylene)bis(1-phenylmethanimine) as the product. These results suggest that the selectivity of metal catalysts for secondary amines depends not only on the properties of the active metals but also on the nature of the COF-carriers, which balance the competition between hydrogenation and amination via fine-tuning the surface chemical environment of the metal catalysts.
 Fig. 50  Pt/COF and other metal catalysts for reductive amination of benzaldehyde. 


3.5.6. Gold-metalated COF catalysts. 
COF-supported gold catalysts and their applications are summarized in Fig. 51, which includes two categories, Au(I) and Au(0).
 Fig. 51  COF-Confined gold catalysts and their applications. 
In 2020, Dong and co-workers reported a COF-supported Au(I)-catalyst.277 The NHC–Au-loaded NHC–AuX-COF (X = Cl−, SbF6−) catalysts can be a highly active reusable catalyst to separately promote the phenylacetylene carboxylation with CO2 and phenylacetylene hydration under mild conditions. In addition, no obvious substituent effect was observed for both carboxylation and hydration reactions, which was evidenced by the generality for the phenylacetylenes with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups.
