Compared with NHC–AuCl-COF with a water contact angle of 123 ± 2°, NHC–AuSbF6-COF is much more hydrophilic (water contact angle was 0°), and thus it is more suitable for catalysing organic reactions in aqueous phase under the applied reaction conditions.
Incorporation of metallocatalysis and organocatalysis into a single platform has attracted extensive research interest for developing undiscovered and valuable organic reactions. In 2022, Dong group reported the COF-catalyst that was concomitant with NHC–Au and chiral amine, viz. (S)-2-methylpiperazine.677 The obtained chiral (S)-NHC–Au-SA-COF is a dual functional catalyst for heterogeneous asymmetric benzyl alcohol oxidation–aldol relay reaction. For example, with the aid of (S)-NHC–Au-SA-COF, 4-(hydroxymethyl)benzonitrile was quantitatively oxidized to 4-formylbenzonitrile under ambient conditions (toluene, air, room temperature). After direct supplementation of acetone and acetic acid, the reaction eventually led to the chiral product (R)-4-(1-hydroxy-3-oxobutyl)benzonitrile. The total yield of the tandem reaction was 89% with 95% ee.
In addition to Au(I), COF-supported Au(0) as nanoparticles for catalysis also appeared in recent literature. For example, with the help of TpPa-1 COF-supported Au(0), p-nitrophenol was completely converted to p-aminophenol in 13 min with NaBH4, which is superior to the performance of HAuCl4 in a homogeneous system.133 The catalytic activity of Au(0) strongly depends on the ratio of atoms on the surface of gold nanoparticles. Au@TpPa-1_HL with a gold particle size of 5 ± 3 nm exhibited a higher catalytic activity than Au@TpPa-1_LL with a gold particle size of 11 ± 4 nm, although the latter had 1.8 times gold loading than the former. Follow-up studies further demonstrated the application of COF-supported Au(0) in organic transformations as heterogeneous catalysts.314,344,345


3.5.7. Silver-metalated COF catalysts. 
Similar to Au(0), nanoscale Ag(0) was also applied in nitrophenol reduction. In 2021, Pan and co-workers fabricated a flow-through reactor by encapsulation of AgNP@SCOF in polyvinyl chloride tubes.320 The reduction reaction of 4-nitrophenol to 4-aminophenol in an aqueous solution of NaBH4 proceeded quantitatively at a reaction solution flow rate of 2000 L (m2 h)−1, and the reduction efficiency decreased to 84% when the flow rate was increased to 4000 L (m2 h)−1.
In addition to reduction reactions, COF-supported Ag(0) nanoparticles can activate unsaturated bonds, promoting the carboxylative cyclization reaction with CO2. As shown by Islam et al., the Ag@TpPa-1-catalysed reaction of prop-2-en-1-amine with CO2 generated 5-(iodomethyl)oxazolidin-2-one in 89% yield under solvent-free conditions at room temperature, in which Ag(0) induced CC activation to form the highly reactive iodonium that was considered a key intermediate for cyclic carbamate formation.678 Furthermore, in a solvent-free system with Ag@TpPa-1 as a catalyst, the carboxylative cyclization reaction of propargylic amines with CO2 resulted in 2-oxazolidinone derivatives in 36–89% yields.678 In 2022, the Bai group examined the catalytic activity of Ag@2,6-FPP-TAPT for the same reaction, and it was found that Ag@2,6-FPP-TAPT exhibited high activity and afforded cyclic carbamates in yields higher than 90% under the given conditions.349 As suggested by the authors, the combined effects of ultra-small Ag(0) nanoparticles, unimpeded mass transfer, and resultful activation of substrates enhanced the activity of Ag@2,6-FPP-TAPT.


3.5.8. Copper-metalated COF catalysts. 
Copper is a promising alternative to precious metals for catalysts due to its low price, diverse catalytic activity, and broad tolerance for functional groups.679,680 Because the copper ion can readily coordinate with N and O heteroatoms, the copper ion-loaded COFs are one of the most common metalated COF catalysts. In addition, Cu(0) can also be loaded in porous COFs as metal nanoparticles for heterogeneous catalysis.
Cu(II)-Loaded COFs can be obtained by a solid-state coordination approach. For example, Dong et al. reported that the reaction of β-ketoacylhydrazone-linked COF-DM with CuCl2 in acetonitrile can afford the Cu(II)-loaded Cu(II)@COF-DM,308 which is a recyclable heterogeneous catalyst for the alkyne–dihalomethane–amine (AHA) coupling reactions. In the presence of Cu(II)@COF-DM and DBU, the reaction of phenylacetylene, dichloromethane and diethylamine in acetonitrile at 80 °C afforded N,N-diethyl-3-phenylprop-2-yn-1-amine in 99% yield (Fig. 52A). As a heterogeneous catalyst, the Cu(II)@COF-DM can be easily recovered by centrifugation, and the yield was still higher than 90% after five catalytic cycles. ICP–OES analysis showed that there was only a tiny amount of Cu(II) loss (approximately 0.7 wt%) after multiple catalytic cycles. In contrast, the Cu(II)-loaded amorphous Cu(II)@polymer-DM provided 92% yield in the first catalytic cycle, but the yield sharply decreased to 16% in its second catalytic cycle.
 Fig. 52  Cu(II)-Anchored COFs as heterogeneous catalysts. (A) Alkyne–dihalomethane–amine coupling reaction. (B) Asymmetric aldehyde–alkyne–amine coupling reaction. (C) Chan–Lam coupling reaction. (D) Copper azide–alkyne cycloaddition reaction. (E) Styrene oxidation. (F) Cycloaddition of epichlorohydrin and CO2. (G) Homocoupling reaction of arylboronic acids. (H) Asymmetric Henry reaction. 
By a similar solid-state coordination approach, the Cu(II)-loaded CCOF-based catalyst can also be synthesized. Very recently, the same group disclosed that CCOFs can be prepared by a catalytic asymmetric polymerization approach.44,681 With the aid of the chiral 2-methylpyrrolidine catalyst, a series of tris(N-salicylideneamine)-derived β-ketoenamine-linked CCOFs were directly synthesized from prochiral aldehyde and primary amine monomers in high yields under ambient conditions.682 The obtained (Λ)- and (Δ)-TpPa-1 with the β-ketoenamine coordination moiety were further metalated by copper(II) trifluoromethanesulfonate in hot ethanol to give the corresponding Cu(II)-metalated CCOFs. Due to the co-existence of Cu(II) and the chiral framework, the generated Cu(II)@(Λ)-TpPa-1 and Cu(II)@(Δ)-TpPa-1 can highly promote asymmetric A3-coupling reactions at room temperature (Fig. 53B). Benefiting from the strong chelating coordination mode between β-ketoenamine and Cu(II), the obtained Cu(II)@(Λ)-TpPa-1 is robust and was reused at least 10 times without loss of its catalytic activity and enantioselectivity. Of special note, a Cu(II)@(Λ)-TpPa-1@chitosan aerogel was also fabricated as a highly efficient fixed-bed model reactor for scaled-up asymmetric A3-coupling reaction.
 Fig. 53  Cu(0)- and Cu(I)-loaded COFs for CO2 fixation. Reaction conditions: (i) CuOx@COF-BD(OH)2, DMF, 25 °C, 10 h; (ii) Cu(0)@TFP-TTA-COF, DBU, acetonitrile, 50 °C, 12 h; (iii) Cu(0)@TFP-TTA-COF, borane dimethylamine complex, ethanol, water, 100 °C, 12 h. 
In 2018, Zhang and co-workers reported a Cu(II)-loaded Cu(CH3COO)2@PI-COF, which is a highly active heterogeneous catalyst that can effectively promote the Chan–Lam coupling reaction of phenylboronic acids with primary amines in air without any base or additive (Fig. 52C).683 In addition, the catalyst can be readily recovered from the reaction system by filtration and reused for at least 8 cycles without any observable change in structure and catalytic activity. The reaction is compatible with a wide range of substrates, and 54 diphenylamine derivatives with different substituents are obtained in 73–92% yields. However, aniline derivatives containing strong electron-withdrawing groups, such as 4-nitroaniline, cannot be converted to the target products. In addition, aliphatic amines, including butan-1-amine, prop-2-en-1-amine, cyclohexanamine, 2-phenylethan-1-amine, and furan-2-ylmethanamine, can be converted to the corresponding secondary amines in 83–90% yields.
Later on, the COF-supported Cu(II)-catalyst was also reported by Wang et al. The obtained Cu(CH3COO)2@TpBpy COF was also employed to catalyse the Chan–Lam coupling reaction but between phenylboronic acid and imidazole (Fig. 52C).231 The reaction was conducted in methanol with triethylamine as the base for 12 h at 70 °C to afford 1-phenyl-1H-imidazole in 66% yield. Examination of the substrate scope led to acceptable reaction yields for 3- and 4-substituted phenylboronic acids, but it was barely able to promote the coupling reaction between 2-substituted phenylboronic acids and imidazole. It is assumed that the 2-substitution increased the steric hindrance, which did not allow the substrates to readily access the catalytically active sites located within the COF pores.
As an example of click chemistry, copper-catalysed azide–alkyne cycloaddition has the advantages of mild conditions and high reaction yields. The catalysts for this reaction are usually Cu(I) or a mixture of Cu(II) and a reductant. In 2020, Gao et al. demonstrated that the click reaction of terminal alkynes with azides can be achieved by a Cu(II)-functionalized COF with sodium ascorbate as the reducing agent.293 For the reaction of phenylacetylene with benzyl azide at 60 °C, the yields in methanol, ethanol, acetonitrile, and water are all above 80%, and the reaction proceeds almost quantitatively in a mixed solvent system of methanol and water. However, the reducing agent used in this reaction cannot be recovered, which increases the cost of the reaction. Thus, supported Cu(II) catalysts without reductants are highly desired.684
In 2021, Zamora and co-workers reported a Cu(II)-decorated β-ketoenamine-linked TAPB-TFP COF by a pre-synthetic metalation method, which was propitious to form the binding amine defects.685 The obtained Cu-TAPB-TFP showed high reactivity in catalysing the reaction of azides with alkynes in the absence of a reducing agent. For example, the reaction of hex-3-yne with (azidomethyl)benzene by Cu-TAPB-TFP afforded 1-benzyl-4,5-diethyl-1H-1,2,3-triazole in 98% yield (Fig. 52D). Importantly, the reaction of asymmetric 4-bromobut-1-yne with (azidomethyl)benzene provided the 1,4-isomer of 1-benzyl-4-(2-bromoethyl)-1H-1,2,3-triazole in 97% yield with 100% regioselectivity, which may be related to the template effect of the COF-pores. This Cu-TAPB-TFP catalyst is stable, and no significant decrease in selectivity nor catalytic activity was observed after five catalytic cycles.
The copper-catalysed olefin oxidation reaction may yield a variety of possible products, such as alcohols, carbonyl compounds, and epoxides. In this context, the selective olefin oxidation is a common topic in current research. In 2017, Chen et al. took advantage of COFs with regular pore channels to embed Cu(II) into imine-linked TAPT-DHTA-COF to form Cu-COFHX by post-synthetic metalation with copper acetate.686 The Cu(II)-loaded Cu-COFHX exhibited good catalytic performance and recyclability towards selective oxidation of styrene to benzaldehyde. As shown in Fig. 52E, in the presence of Cu-COFHX, the styrene oxidation afforded benzaldehyde and styrene oxide with 76% conversion at a molar ratio of 38:7. However, for (E)-1,2-diphenylethene and hex-1-ene, the epoxides were the main products. Furthermore, (Z)-cyclooctene mainly underwent α-H oxidation to yield (Z)-cyclooct-2-en-1-one. This selective oxidation reaction is closely related to the substrate size and the Cu(II) location in the COF-framework.
Other types of catalytic reactions by COF-supported Cu(II) have also been reported. For example, Ma et al. reported that Cu(II)-doped COF-TpBpy containing a polymeric phosphonium salt in the pores can convert a variety of oxirane derivatives, such as 2-(chloromethyl)oxirane, 2-ethyloxirane, 2-((allyloxy)methyl)oxirane, and 2-(phenoxymethyl)oxirane, to the corresponding cyclic carbonates in 1 atm of CO2 with greater than 90% yields (Fig. 52F).232 In addition, the CuO nanoparticle-loaded TRIPTA COF for coupling of phenylboronic acids to biphenyls was also reported by Bhaumik and co-workers (Fig. 52G).370 Also, the Cu(II)-grafted (Δ)-TpTab-Cu CCOF catalyst, which was prepared by a combined approach of chiral induction and post-synthetic metalation, for asymmetric Henry reactions was reported by Cui et al. in 2018 (Fig. 52H).304 In the presence of (Δ)-TpTab-Cu and N-ethyl-N-isopropylpropan-2-amine, the reaction between nitromethane and 4-bromobenzaldehyde in mesitylene at −10 °C for 48 h afforded the corresponding β-nitroalcohol with 10% conversion of aldehyde and 35% ee.
In addition to the organic conversions catalysed by Cu(II)-COFs, the catalytic properties of low-valent copper (Cu(I) and Cu(0)) have also been reported. In 2021, Gao and co-workers reported a Cu(I)-decorated TpBpy COF, which is demonstrated to be a good platform for CO2 adsorption and transformation.236 For example, the TpBpy-Cu-catalysed ethynylbenzene carboxylation with CO2 afforded 3-phenylpropiolic acid in 95% yield. The same year, Li and co-workers reported that the Cu(I)-based JNM-1 COF can efficiently catalyse the Sonogashira cross-coupling reactions of phenylacetylene with aryl iodides in DMF.263 Specifically, no obvious substituent effect was observed in this reaction, and the aryl iodides with either electron-withdrawing or electron-donating functional groups are executable with the product yields ranging from 90 to 97%. Moreover, IISERP-COF9-supported Cu/Cu2O nanoparticles for Glaser–Hay coupling between two terminal alkynes to asymmetric dienes were reported by Vaidhyanathan et al.360 The oxidation of Cu(0)/Cu(I) to Cu(II)/Cu(III) was observed in the reaction, and the formed copper(III) diacetylide complex was considered the key intermediate.
In 2020, Islama et al. reported a low-valent Cu/CuxOy nanoparticle-loaded COF for CO2 transformation via carboxylative cyclization. As shown in Fig. 53, the synthesized Cu@COF-BD(OH)2 could catalyse the three-component one-pot reaction of (Z)-2-bromo-3-phenylacrylic acid, aniline, and CO2 in DMF to afford (Z)-5-benzylidene-3-phenyloxazolidine-2,4-dione in 96% yield at room temperature.324 In addition, TFP-TTA-COF-supported copper nanoparticles for carboxylative cyclization of N-benzylprop-2-yn-1-amine and cyclization of benzene-1,2-diamine with CO2 were reported by the same group in the same year.358 Both examples of catalysts were reusable at least six times, emphasizing the role of COFs in stabilizing copper-based nanoparticles.


3.5.9. Iron-metalated COF catalysts. 
Similar to copper-based complexes, iron-based complexes are also a class of cheap and readily available catalysts,687 and they have been widely used to catalyse the cross-coupling reactions, asymmetric transfer hydrogenation, CO2 chemical transformation, etc.688 To date, several impressive examples that highlight the application of Fe-metalated COFs in organic transformations have appeared in the literature.
In 2017, Cui et al. first applied the Fe-metalated COF catalyst for asymmetric epoxidation of olefins.297 In the presence of Fe(II)-loaded CCOF4-Fe as the catalyst with iodosylbenzene as the oxidant, 2,2-dimethyl-2H-chromene and spiro[chromene-2,1′-cyclohexane] were successfully oxidized to (1aS,7bS)-2,2-dimethyl-1a,7b-dihydro-2H-oxireno[2,3-c]chromene (76% conversion and 90% ee) and 1a′,7b′-dihydrospiro[cyclohexane-1,2′-oxireno[2,3-c]chromene] (84% conversion with 94% ee) in chloroform at −20 °C. However, with NaClO as the oxidant, only a trace amount of the desired products was obtained.
In 2018, Buarque et al. reported an iron-based COF catalyst, FeCl3@TPB-DMTP-COF, which could effectively facilitate decarboxylative reactions of cinnamic acids.689 Using di-tert-butyl peroxide as the oxidant, the reaction of cinnamic acid with dioxane at 120 °C for 6 h yielded (E)-2-styryl-1,4-dioxane in 86% yield. The reaction was compatible with methoxy, hydroxyl, and methyl groups in 28–79% yields. Interestingly, only (E)-isomers were obtained, whether (Z)- or (E)-cinnamic acid derivatives were used, because of the free radical mechanism.
One year later, Fang et al. reported a β-ketoenamine-linked COF which can trap Fe(II) into the COF-framework via the chelating coordination.690 The generated JUC-521-Fe served as the Fenton catalyst to degrade rhodamine 6G with a degradation rate of up to 95% in 90 min.
In 2020, Tang et al. reported a Fe(III)-embedded COF by defective engineering of COF-LZU1, and the obtained LZU-OFe exhibited excellent catalytic activity in epoxide alcoholysis.270 As shown, 2-phenyloxirane could be quantitatively converted to 2-methoxy-2-phenylethan-1-ol with the aid of LZU-OFe at room temperature. As a heterogeneous catalyst, LZU-OFe was reused three times without obvious performance loss.


3.5.10. Cobalt-metalated COF catalysts. 
Since the discovery of hydroformylation reactions, cobalt has been recognized as an efficient catalyst for a variety of organic reactions, including cycloadditions, borohydride reductions, and hydrosilylations.691 It has been demonstrated that introducing electron-rich units, e.g., porphyrin, cyclopentadienyl, and salen, into COF-scaffolds can be attractive for COFs to immobilize cobalt ions. To date, the reported oxidation states of cobalt in Co-metalated COFs are 0, +2, and +3. Co(-I) and Co(I) have not been incorporated into COFs, although their related organometallic complexes have been reported for homogeneous catalysis.692
Co(II), as a Lewis acid, can effectively activate epoxides and promote their ring-opening addition with CO2. For example, Gao et al. reported a Co-metalated TF-TACo-COF by the reaction of cobalt(II) 5,10,15,20-tetrakis(4-aminophenyl)porphyrin and 4,4′,4′′,4′′′-(pyrene-1,3,6,8-tetrayl)tetrabenzaldehyde in a mixed solvent of N,N-dimethylacetamide, 1,4-dioxane, and mesitylene at 140 °C for 3 days.693 The cobalt porphyrin units in TF-TACo-COF are alternately stacked along the crystallographic c-axis with a layer spacing of 3.8 Å, and the two adjacent layers in the ab-plane have a slip of 1.7 Å. Compared with the classical AA and AB stacking, this unique stacking form fully exposes the Co(II) catalytic sites, ensuring the smooth progress of CO2 cycloaddition with 2-(chloromethyl)oxirane. With the aid of tetrabutylammonium iodide, the reaction yield at 313 K is over 90% even with CO2 pressure as low as 0.1 MPa. Large substrates, such as 2-phenyloxirane, 2-hexyloxirane, and 2-(butoxymethyl)oxirane, are difficult to contact the Co(II) sites in the COF-matrix, resulting in low reaction yields. Similarly, Co-BD-COF reported by Chen et al. containing a (η4-tetraphenylcyclobutadiene)(η5-cyclopentadienyl)cobalt(II) sandwich complex also exhibited high activity in catalytic CO2 cycloaddition with epoxides to produce cyclic carbonates.694
In addition to direct polymerization, the Co(II) ions can also be loaded by COFs via the post-synthetic metalation. In 2017, Wu et al. reported a Co(II)-loaded porphyrin-COF by heating the 3D imine-linked CPF-2 with CoCl2 in DMF. The generated Co-CPF-2 COF under ambient conditions exhibits efficient heterogeneous catalytic activity in the aerobic epoxidation of olefins.695 In oxygen-rich acetonitrile, a variety of olefins (e.g., styrene, cyclopentene, cyclohexene, (Z)-cyclooctene, and hex-1-ene) were effectively oxidized to the corresponding epoxides in the presence of isobutyraldehyde. The yield was negatively correlated with the molecular size of the substrate, indicating an intrapore catalytic process. Mechanistic studies suggest that the (CH3)2CHCO˙ radical formed by the reaction of isobutyraldehyde and Co(II) is a key intermediate for the activation of molecular oxygen.
Similarly, Co-metalation can also be achieved on salen-based COFs. In 2017, Hoskins et al. reported a Co/salen-COF that was prepared by Co(II)-metalation of an established salen-COF via a post-synthetic approach.295 The obtained Co/salen-COF can catalyse the Henry reaction in a heterogeneous way. In addition to the Co(II)-metalated COF, the Co(III)-metalated COF has also been realized by Yang and co-workers in 2019, and the obtained COF-salen-Co(III) was applied in epoxide hydration successfully.296
The Co-involved nanoparticles can also be loaded into porous COFs. In 2018, Vaidhyanathan and co-workers demonstrated that the nitrogen-rich COFs can support and stabilize Co/Co(OH)2 nanoparticles.361 The cobalt nanoparticle-loaded Co@IISERP-COF5 can catalyse the reaction of NaBH4 with methanol under basic conditions to produce hydrogen, which can be in situ utilized for the hydrogenation of nitrile and nitro compounds to amines under mild conditions in a facile one-pot reaction. In contrast, the free Co/Co(OH)2 nanoparticles were less active, implying that the nitrogen-rich binding pockets in IISERP-COF5 ensure strong interactions between the Co/Co(OH)2 nanoparticles and the COF-carrier, which provides high catalytic activity and enables catalyst recycling.


3.5.11. Nickel-metalated COF catalysts. 
Due to its low-cost and relatively large earth abundance, nickel(II) is an ideal alternative to palladium for Suzuki–Miyaura coupling reaction. In 2020, Esteves et al. tested the potential of NiCl@RIO-12 as a heterogeneous catalyst in the cross-coupling reaction of 1-(4-bromophenyl)ethan-1-one and phenylboronic acid in toluene with the aid of K3PO4.291 In a microwave reactor (180 °C, 2.45 GHz), a 34% conversion to 1-([1,1′-biphenyl]-4-yl)ethan-1-one was obtained after 15 min. Although the reaction efficacy is far from that of the Pd-based counterpart, this work is still a valuable exploration of Ni-metalated COFs for catalytic research.
In addition, the Ni(0)-loaded dehydrobenzoannulene-based COF, which has a nickel content of 8.56 wt%, was reported in 2020 by McGrier and co-workers.283 The Ni-metalation therein was achieved by combining an azine-linked 2D COF with bis(1,5-cyclooctadiene)nickel(0) in toluene at room temperature. The obtained Ni-DBA-2D-COF is a de facto nickel single-atom catalyst that can highly catalyze the desulfurization of aryl thioethers which was evidenced by the reaction of methyl(naphthalen-2-yl)sulfane to naphthalene (ethyldimethylsilane, toluene, 130 °C, 87% yield). Of note, Ni-DBA-2D-COF exhibited recoverability over five catalytic cycles with negligible leakage of Ni and almost constant catalytic activity. The mechanistic study showed that upon heating, Ni(0) broke two coordination bonds, underwent oxidative addition to insert into the C–S bond, followed by σ-bond metathesis with ethyldimethylsilane, and finally reductive elimination to obtain the product and complete catalyst regeneration.
Later, Ma et al. reported an additional Ni(0)-loaded COF catalyst by roasting the nickel acetate-embedded TpDb COF in a H2/Ar atmosphere at 350 °C.357 The obtained H350-Ni/COF enables selective hydrogenation of phenylacetylene with greater than 99% conversion and up to 85% selectivity for styrene. Both the conversion rate and the selectivity are superior to those of the TiO2-supported Ni, C3N4-supported Ni, and commercial Raney Ni catalysts. This H350-Ni/COF-catalyzed semihydrogenation reaction was demonstrated to be also suitable for other substituted phenylacetylenes.
Therefore, Ni-metalated COFs are a promising class of non-precious metallic catalysts for application in demanding organic transformations for fine chemical products because the cheap abundant metal-based reusable catalysts are highly significant for a sustainable society.


3.5.12. Zinc-metalated COF catalysts. 
As an additional cheap abundant metal, Zn is also selected as a handy metal source to conduct COF metalation. In 2022, Islam et al. grafted Zn(II) on a N-rich COF to obtain a Zn-metalated COF.696 The obtained Zn@TpTta exhibits good catalytic activity in the fixation of CO2 under sustainable conditions. The corresponding cyclic carbonates were generated in 69–99% yields in 1 atm CO2 under solvent-free conditions. Moreover, Zn@TpTta is also highly active in facilitating atmospheric cyclizative CO2 capture, which led to the formation of diverse iodo-substituted cyclic carbamates in 61–94% yields from unsaturated amines using N-iodosuccinimide as an electrophilic reagent under optimized conditions.


3.5.13. Manganese-metalated COF catalysts. 
Mn–salen molecular complexes are effective catalysts for olefin epoxidation. However, the formation of μ-oxo-Mn dimers is a potential deactivation pathway for the Mn–salen catalysts. The immobilization of Mn–salen complexes in the porous COF supports has been demonstrated to be a facile and effective way to prevent the dimer formation. In 2019, the Yang group developed a post-synthetic protocol for the preparation of COF-salen–Mn, and moreover, successfully realized its catalytic application in styrene epoxidation (t-butyl hydroperoxide, acetonitrile, 40 °C, 24 h, 74% conversion).296 In contrast, the homogeneous Mn–salen catalyst afforded much slower reaction rate than COF-salen–Mn, and only approximately 58% conversion of styrene was achieved under the same reaction conditions.


3.5.14. Vanadium-metalated COF catalysts. 
Different from the most metalated COFs, the introduction of vanadium into COFs is usually in the form of vanadium oxide cations. For example, in 2019, Ma et al. utilized an imine-linked COF containing a catecholate moiety to trap VO2+ ions by a single step reaction of TAPT-2,3-DHTA with bis(2,4-pentanedionato)vanadium(IV) oxide.267 The obtained oxovanadium-functionalized VO-TAPT-2,3-DHTA has the ability to catalyse a series of organic transformations, including the Prins reaction,267tert-butyl hydroperoxide-assisted sulphide oxidation,267 and Mannich-type reaction268 (Fig. 54).
 Fig. 54  Oxovanadium(IV)-catalyzed Prins carbonyl–ene reaction, oxidation reaction of thioethers, and modified Mannich-type reaction. 


3.5.15. Molybdenum-metalated COF catalysts. 
By a similar post-synthetic protocol, Jiang et al. prepared a β-ketoacylhydrazone-linked COF containing a bis(acetylacetonato)dioxomolybdenum(VI) moiety.309 The molybdenum-doped Mo-COF with a Mo content of 2.0 mmol g−1 can be an open nanochannel-reactor for the selective epoxidation reaction of olefins. With the aid of tert-butyl hydroperoxide, Mo-COF promoted the cyclohexene epoxidation to 7-oxabicyclo[4.1.0]heptane with a conversion rate above 99% and a selectivity of 77%, and (Z)-cyclooctene epoxidation to 9-oxabicyclo[6.1.0]nonane with a conversion rate of 80% and a selectivity higher than 99%. For the alkene substrates with long alkyl chains such as oct-1-ene and dodec-1-ene, the catalytic reactions provided below 20% conversion but with selectivity above 99%, suggesting that the Mo-COF-catalysed epoxidation is an inner pore catalytic process.


3.5.16. Heterometallic COF catalysts. 
Because COFs have multiple possible coordination sites, it is logically feasible to load two or more types of metals into specific positions within the COF-scaffolds. Such heterometallic COF catalysts provide an opportunity for tandem reactions. In 2022, Gao and co-workers reported a Py-2,2′-BPyPh COF which contains two possible coordination sites of 2,2′-bipyridine and imine bonds.244 After sequentially doping Mn(II) and Pd(II) in the Py-2,2′-BPyPh COF, a heterobimetallic Mn/Pd@Py-2,2′-BPyPh COF was obtained, and it can promote a Heck–epoxidation tandem reaction, in which the loaded Pd(II) and Mn(II) are responsible for Heck addition and epoxidation reaction, respectively. Thereafter, the same group reported additional heterobimetallic Pd(II)/Cu(II)@TADP-COF which can catalyse a coupling–oxidation cascade reaction in water.697
Furthermore, chloro(1,5-cyclooctadiene)rhodium(I) and palladium acetate were incorporated into a 75%-BPy-COF via a two-step reaction and used to catalyze the reaction of phenylboronic acid and benzaldehyde to obtain benzophenone without the need to separate the diphenylmethanol intermediate.698
By integrating two metal nanoparticles of Pd and Au into Phos-COF-1, tandem Suzuki–Miyaura coupling and nitro reduction reactions have been achieved using a one-pot method, leading to [1,1′-biphenyl]-4-amine from phenylboronic acid and 1-bromo-4-nitrobenzene.271 The oxidation state of Au increased from 0 to +3, and the oxidation state of P decreased, raising doubts about the reusability of the catalyst.

3.6. Sensing and analysis
The colossal specific surface area of COFs makes them easy to interact with the external environment, which has been exploited to develop sensing materials.699 Based on pore engineering of COFs,700 the pore walls can be precisely designed to selectively interact with target analytes, thus allowing the design of various types of chemical detection systems based on the principles of colorimetry,701,702 chemiluminescence,336,703 photoluminescence,704–706 chemoelectrical transduction,707 chromatography,708,709 and others to achieve highly sensitive and selective detection.
In addition to being used for the detection of metal ions, the metalated COFs offer several advantages for the detection of other types of analytes. For example, iron-metalated COFs can trigger Fenton reaction for decomposing H2O2 to yield ˙OH that can be combined with a variety of chromogenic and luminescent substrates (e.g., 3,3′,5,5′-tetramethylbenzidine, o-diaminobenzene, 9-ethyl-9H-carbazol-3-amine, and luminol) to achieve colorimetric and chemiluminescence detection. Obviously, the non-enzymatic detection system based on iron-metalated COFs possesses better stability than the conventional horseradish peroxidase-based detection system. In addition, COFs loaded with rare earth ions have rich photochemistry with potential applications in fluorescence sensing. Also, COF-supported noble metal nanoparticles (e.g., Pt, Au, and Ag) can be used for electrochemical sensing with enhanced electrical conductivity. COFs, as crystalline porous carriers, not only provide channels for the directional and rapid diffusion of analytes but also protect the metal nanoparticles from aggregation and accidental oxidative deactivation.
This section will comprehensively summarize the sensing applications of metalated COFs.

3.6.1. Colorimetry. 
Ferrous-metalated COFs have been developed for the colorimetric detection of H2O2 and glucose. On the basis of the porphyrin–Fe(II) coordination, Dai et al. reported an Fe(II)-decorated COF-366-Fe in 2018 (Fig. 55).710 In a sodium acetate buffer solution of pH = 5.0, the authors demonstrated that COF-366-Fe could promote H2O2 decomposition via Fenton-like reaction to produce ˙OH, which in turn caused 3,3′,5,5′-tetramethylbenzidine to change from colorless to blue. Kinetic analysis showed that the Michaelis–Menten constant Km of COF-366-Fe was lower than that of natural horseradish peroxidase, implying that the affinity of COF-366-Fe for 3,3′,5,5′-tetramethylbenzidine and H2O2 was stronger than that of natural peroxidase. During the assay, Fe(II) did not leak out, confirming its good chemical stability. Based on this colorimetric reaction, COF-366-Fe was used to detect H2O2 in solution at a detection limit of 1.1 μM. Furthermore, since glucose and oxygen can be catalysed by glucose oxidase to produce gluconic acid accompanied by H2O2 production, COF-366-Fe was used in combination with glucose oxidase to specifically detect glucose at a detection limit of 1.0 μM. Other common carbohydrates, such as sucrose, maltose, and lactose, did not interfere with glucose determination. For human serum samples, the results obtained by the above colorimetric method based on COF-366-Fe were consistent with those of commercial blood glucose meters. Furthermore, through the coordination reaction of FeSO4 with imine bonds, Fe(II) was embedded in RT-COF-1 for the detection of H2O2 at a detection limit of 5.6 μM and a linear range of 10–2000 μM.711 Fe-RT-COF-1 was used to detect H2O2 in dairy products with a recovery of 96.3–100.7%.
 Fig. 55  Iron(II)-coordinated COF-366-Fe for the colorimetric detection of H2O2 and glucose. (A) Structure of COF-366-Fe. (B) Principle of colorimetric detection of H2O2 and glucose with 3,3′,5,5′-tetramethylbenzidine as the chromogenic agent. 
Recently, noble metal nanoparticles have been reported as iron-free Fenton-like reaction catalysts for colorimetric analysis. In 2019, Yan et al. developed gold nanoparticle-loaded PTAzo-COF-AuNP for the colorimetric analysis of Hg(II).337 In the presence of Hg(II), PTAzo-COF-AuNP catalysed the reaction of H2O2 with the chromogenic substrate of 3,3′,5,5′-tetramethylbenzidine, resulting in an enhanced absorption at 652 nm. In buffer solution at pH = 4, PTAzo-COF-AuNP achieved an ultrasensitive colorimetric analysis of Hg(II) with a detection limit of 0.75 nM and a linear range of 5–300 nM with excellent analytical robustness. Effectively, the colorimetric method is highly versatile, as it combines different chromogenic substrates. Very recently, Pt@Tz-Da@GOx that was decorated by Pt nanoparticles and glucose oxidase was prepared by Jiang and co-workers via a stepwise post-treatment of the Tz-Da COF.330 In a buffer solution at pH = 8, glucose oxidase converts glucose to gluconic acid and H2O2; Pt subsequently catalyzes the reaction of phenol with the chromogenic substrate 4-aminoantipyrine in the presence of H2O2, causing the solution to turn red. The absorbance at 505 nm is linearly related to the phenol concentration in the range of 12.5–300 μM. As a carrier, the Tz-Da COF protects the activity of ultrasmall platinum (2–3 nm) and glucose oxidase from external environmental damage.


3.6.2. Chemiluminescence sensing. 
Ferric- and ferrous-coordinated COFs can accelerate the oxidation of luminol by triggering H2O2 decomposition, resulting in pronounced chemiluminescence. As indicated by Li et al., in the carbonate buffer solution with pH = 11, the chemiluminescence caused by mixing luminol, H2O2 and COF-366-Fe is 27 times stronger than that of the COF-free system.703 In addition, the chemiluminescence intensity is linearly related to the H2O2 concentration in the range of 0.01–10.0 μM with a detection limit of 5.3 nM. Through the reaction of glucose and glucose oxidase to generate H2O2in situ, COF-366-Fe can also be used in the chemiluminescence assay of glucose, with a linear range of 0.05–8.0 μM and a detection limit of 4 nM.


3.6.3. Fluorescence sensing. 
Many COFs are weakly fluorescent or nonfluorescent due to π–π stacking between layers and rotation and vibration of covalent bonds within the layers.712–716 Several design strategies have been shown to improve their fluorescence quantum yield. For example, photoluminescent COFs can be synthesized by introducing large π-conjugated structures, such as pyrene,717–719 porphyrin,190,720–722 triazatruxene,723 and triphenylene,724 into monomers. In these COFs, the large π-conjugation enhances the probability of the π–π* transition; thus, this transition is easily excited and is accompanied by a redshift of the maximum absorption and emission wavelengths. In addition, the use of intralayer hydrogen bonds and steric effects to limit the free rotation of the linkages and the vibration of O–H and N–H bonds can reduce the energy dissipation caused by nonradiative transitions and improve the fluorescence intensity.725–728 Third, modulating the interlayer stacking interactions is also an effective way to optimize the fluorescence properties of 2D COFs: (i) the introduction of bulky substituents, such as tert-butyl,729 into the monomer increases the interlayer spacing and weakens the fluorescence quenching caused by interlayer interactions; (ii) AB stacking of 2D COFs can minimize the interlayer interactions while maintaining the crystalline structure, which is beneficial for fluorescence enhancement;730–733 (iii) exfoliating bulk 2D COFs into nanosheets or quantum dots can reduce the number of stacked layers, thereby enhancing fluorescence;734,735 and (iv) the introduction of twisted intralayer structures can weaken the interlayer stacking interactions of 2D COFs, accompanied by fluorescence enhancement.419,736,737 Furthermore, monomers with aggregation-induced emission properties, such as 1,1,2,2-tetraphenylethene,723,738,739 are beneficial for the synthesis of 2D COFs with high fluorescence quantum yields. Furthermore, π–π stacking interactions in 3D COFs are usually weak or absent, and thus they are a promising class of candidates for the construction of fluorescent COFs.81,740 Finally, photoluminescence properties beyond single-photon fluorescence, such as two-photon fluorescence, have recently been disclosed in some COFs with a wide range of π-conjugated systems and strong intralayer charge transfer.741–745
In addition, metal ions can also affect the fluorescence properties of COFs through various mechanisms. For example, metal ions can cause fluorescence quenching of the metalated COFs through several mechanisms: (i) enhancing absorption competition,746–748 (ii) promoting photoinduced electron transfer,307,729,736,749 (iii) blocking intramolecular charge transfer,750 and (iv) interrupting the π-conjugated structure.751 On the other hand, metal-assisted fluorescence enhancement mechanisms usually include (i) blocking photoinduced electron transfer,752,753 (ii) limiting covalent bond rotation,754 and (iii) weakening interlayer stacking.755 Thus, metalation of COFs provides an effective means of modulating the fluorescence properties of COFs as needed for fluorescence sensing.
The metalated COF-based fluorescence visualization for intracellular H2S was reported very recently. In 2021, Du and co-workers reported a copper(II) porphyrin-based CuPor-COF for sensitive and selective detection of H2S.756 As indicated by the authors, H2S could react with Cu(II) and release the metal-free COF to turn on the fluorescence (Fig. 56A). Upon excitation with 419 nm light, the fluorescence increased at 700 nm, and the detection limit was as low as 10 nM (Fig. 56B). In addition, CuPor-COF has good selectivity and is almost free from the interference of common anions and biological thiols.
 Fig. 56  Demetalation of metalated COFs for fluorescence sensing. (A) CuPor-COF for the fluorescence detection of H2S. (B) Fluorescence spectra of CuPor-COF with different concentrations of NaHS. Adapted with permission of ref. 756. Copyright © 2021 Wiley-VCH GmbH. (C) TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) for the fluorescence detection of ATP. (D) Fluorescence spectra of TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) with different concentrations of ATP. (E) TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) for confocal fluorescence imaging of intracellular ATP. Ca(II) was used to promote endogenous ATP production. Adapted with permission of ref. 748. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society. 
In 2021, Huang et al. found that Fe(III) can bind to the imine linkage of TPB-DMTP-COF through metal–linkage coordination, forming TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) and quenching the fluorescence at 515 nm, while adenosine triphosphate (ATP) has the ability to bind Fe(III) through the Fe–O–P bond, leading to the removal of Fe(III) from TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) accompanied with fluorescence recovery.748 Therefore, TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) is a probe for ATP detection (Fig. 56C). Experimental results indicated that the fluorescence of TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) was enhanced along with the increase in the amount of ATP in aqueous solution, and the detection limit was 2.4 nM (Fig. 56D). High levels of proteins, amino acids, and inorganic ions barely interfered with the detection. Based on the obtained fluorescence properties and good biocompatibility, TPB-DMTP-COF-Fe(III) has been further used for fluorescence imaging of intracellular ATP and track Ca(II)-induced intracellular ATP upregulation in real time (Fig. 56E).
Among the metalated COFs, lanthanide-immobilized COFs have received additional attention. The fluorescence emissions of lanthanide ions potentially cover a wide range from the ultraviolet to near-infrared regions due to their numerous energy levels originating from f–f transitions; they have unique photoluminescence features, such as sharp emission bands, large Stokes’ shifts, and long luminescence lifetimes, creating a vast library of fluorescent probe candidates.757,758 However, the fluorescence of hydrated lanthanide ions is usually weak due to weak light absorption. The antenna effect,759 which achieves energy absorption and transfer by means of ligands in the excited triplet state that have the same energy levels as lanthanide ions, effectively enhances fluorescence emission. This effect is essentially a form of intramolecular energy transfer from the organic ligands to the lanthanide ions, and it can be regulated by ligand exchanges, thereby inducing fluorescence enhancement or quenching and achieving sensitive fluorescence sensing. Based on this principle, lanthanide-metalated COFs have been developed as fluorescence sensors (Fig. 57).
 Fig. 57  Eu(III)- and Tb(III)-Metalated COFs as turn-on fluorescent sensors. *TAA, 2-thenoyltrifluoroacetone. 
The Eu(III)-functionalized TpPa-1 COF was used to detect levofloxacin, a quinolone antibiotic, as reported by Yan et al.760 TpPa-1 cannot enhance the fluorescence emission of Eu(III) via the antenna effect, and Eu@TpPa-1 and TpPa-1 have similar photoluminescence properties because the triplet energy level of TpPa-1 (15504 cm−1) is lower than the 5D0 energy level of Eu(III) (17500 cm−1). The coordination of the β-diketone moiety in levofloxacin and Eu(III) affects the energy transfer, thus enabling the fluorescence detection of levofloxacin. In the presence of high concentrations of levofloxacin, the excited singlet state of levofloxacin is transformed into the excited triplet state via intersystem crossing, and the energy is then transferred from the lowest energy level of the excited triplet state to the excited energy level of Eu(III), effectively sensitizing the Eu(III) emission and leading to a significant enhancement of its 5D0 → 7FJ (J = 1, 2, 3, 4) emission at 592, 613, 652, and 701 nm. When there is a low concentration of levofloxacin, the sensitization of the Eu(III) emission is limited; however, the levofloxacin ligand occupies the site of the original water ligand on Eu(III), attenuating the nonradiative transition due to thermal vibrations and continuing to enhance the fluorescence. As a result, the levofloxacin induces an enhancement of Eu@TpPa-1 fluorescence in a concentration-dependent manner at a detection limit of 0.2 μM and within a linear range from 1 μM to 10 mM. The fluorescence sensing property of Eu@TpPa-1 is robust and has been applied in the detection of levofloxacin in serum and urine. In addition, based on the same detection mechanism, the Eu(III)-modified TyBpy COF was also reported by Liu and co-workers for fluorescence detection of levofloxacin with a detection limit of 0.51 μM and a linear range of 2–800 μM.761
The introduction of carboxyl groups on the pore walls of COFs can increase the coordination sites of lanthanide ions and further improve the detection sensitivity. This year, Song et al. prepared a DTA-TFP COF containing carboxyl groups using 2,4,6-trihydroxybenzene-1,3,5-tricarbaldehyde and 2,5-diaminoterephthalic acid.762 After loading Eu(III) through the coordination bond, the Eu@DTA-TFP exhibited weak fluorescence due to energy level mismatch. Pyridine-2,6-dicarboxylic acid, an organic ligand with multiple coordinating atoms, can enhance the fluorescence of Eu(III) at 592, 615, and 693 nm through the antenna effect but has almost no effect on the fluorescence of the DTA-TFP COF at 457 nm. Therefore, using the fluorescence emission at 457 nm as an internal reference and the enhanced emission at 615 nm, ratiometric fluorescence detection of pyridine-2,6-dicarboxylic acid with a detection limit of 4.2 nM within a linear range of 0.01–12.0 μM has been achieved. Tb@DTA-TFP, which contains Tb(III) instead of Eu(III), can achieve the ratiometric fluorescence detection of pyridine-2,6-dicarboxylic acid at 544 nm with a detection limit of 2.9 nM within a linear range of 0.01–9.0 μM.
To construct turn-off fluorescent probes, Yan et al. prepared Eu-TAA@TpDq with bright red emission by introducing the additional β-diketone ligand 2-thenoyltrifluoroacetone (TAA) into the Eu(III)-coordinated TpDq COF.763 Common aldehydes, including formaldehyde, acetaldehyde, propionaldehyde, butyraldehyde, benzaldehyde, and glutaraldehyde, quenched the fluorescence of Eu-TAA@TpDq to varying degrees. Among them, glutaraldehyde diminished the fluorescence intensity at 613 nm to 1/398 of the initial value, successfully realizing the fluorescence sensing of glutaraldehyde at a detection limit of 4.55 μM. Mechanistically, this fluorescence quenching was attributed to dynamic quenching; the collision of the excited Eu-TAA@TpDq with glutaraldehyde resulted in energy dissipation. Unfortunately, Eu-TAA@TpDq could not detect glutaraldehyde in water because the water quenched the Eu(III) fluorescence. Based on the same principle of TAA-enhanced Eu(III) photoluminescence, in 2022, a TpBD-(COOH)2@Eu-TTA COF hydrogel was used for the simultaneous detection of vanilacetic acid and Al(III), both of which are associated with neurodegenerative diseases.764 In aqueous solution, due to competitive energy absorption and dynamic quenching, vanilacetic acid led to fluorescence quenching at 614 nm, while Al(III) led to fluorescence quenching at 614 nm and enhancement at 412 nm. The detection limits for vanilacetic acid and Al(III) were 132 nM and 23.4 nM, respectively. However, the partial leaching of Eu(III) during the detection process limited the reusability of the TpBD-(COOH)2@Eu-TTA COF.
Finally, it is worth mentioning that temperature has a significant effect on the photoluminescence properties of lanthanide ions, enabling temperature sensing. By post-treatment of the TpBpy COF with rare earth acetylacetonate complexes under solvothermal conditions, Carlos and co-workers prepared TpBpy-Ln (Ln = Eu, Tb, Dy) with strong fluorescence emission.146 The acetylacetonate co-ligands in these metalated COFs not only prevent the fluorescence quenching caused by water molecules but also act as an antenna ligand to sensitize the fluorescence emission of rare earth elements. Variable-temperature fluorescence spectra showed that TpBpy-Eu/Tb, which is anchored with Eu(III) and Tb(III) ions, has temperature-dependent fluorescence emission. In the temperature range of 10–360 K, due to metal-to-ligand energy back-transfer, the lifetime of the Eu(III) 5D0 energy level decreases with increasing temperature, which leads to a negative correlation between the 610 nm emission intensity and temperature. However, because of the mismatch between the T1 energy level of TpBpy COF and the 5D4 energy level of Tb(III), there is no such thermally activated emission decay of Tb(III), and the 548 nm fluorescence emission intensity is almost independent of temperature. This gives TpBpy-Eu/Tb the potential to be a ratiometric fluorescence thermometer. In addition, the characteristic emission peaks of TpBpy-Dy, such as 4F9/2 → 6H15/2 transition, have temperature-dependent photoluminescence properties in the temperature range of 280–440 K. TpBpy-Dy is also a candidate for a fluorescence thermometer. In 2021, Kaczmarek et al. also reported the use of a TTA-DFP COF loaded with terbium(III) trifluoroacetylacetonate or europium(III) trifluoroacetylacetonate as a fluorescence thermometer.765 Eu(III) and Tb(III) were bound to the pyridin-2-ylmethanimino moiety. Within the range of 273–403 K, the emission intensities of Tb(III) at 544 nm and Eu(III) at 612 nm decreased with increasing temperature. However, the thermal quenching process of the 544 nm emission had a lower activation energy than that of the 612 nm emission, and thus, the decrease in the intensity of the 544 nm fluorescence peak was more pronounced, allowing the Eu(III)- and Tb(III)-combined TTA-DFP COF to be used as a ratiometric fluorescence thermometer.
Despite numerous advances in this type of sensing, metalated COF fluorescent probes still face several limitations that prevent them from replacing existing chromatographic methods, including selectivity, reusability, and sample preparation. First, the stability of metalated COF dispersions is a major challenge for the detection. In the detection medium, the aggregation of metalated COF particles leads to fluorescence decay, which directly interferes with the analyte-induced fluorescence enhancement or quenching and compromises the long-term stability of the detection system. A feasible solution is to prepare nanoscale metalated COFs, such as quantum dots, nanosheets, and nanospheres.179 In addition, fluorescent probes constructed based on energy transfer processes rather than demetalation reactions face a significant limitation of lack of specificity. It is not easy to distinguish between two structurally similar compounds, especially when structural analogs coexist. Although some potential interferents have been explored in existing research, the structural analogs have not been fully screened and their mechanisms have not been adequately studied. Furthermore, the detection media of metalated COF fluorescent probes are usually organic solvents, and the excitation wavelengths are below 400 nm, both limiting in vivo and in vitro fluorescence detection.766


3.6.4. Electroanalysis. 
Electroanalysis sensors perform qualitative and quantitative determinations of substances by converting chemical quantities (e.g., concentration) into electrical quantities (e.g., current, potential, and resistance of charge transfer), which are usually based on functionalized modified electrodes and the corresponding redox reactions.767 The classical materials applied in electrochemical sensing are 2D materials represented by graphene, metal oxides, and MXenes, which have the advantages of fully exposed active sites, anisotropic carrier migration, and abundant surface chemistry.768 Recently, 2D COFs have shown potential in electrochemical analysis because they do not have many of the limitations of previous 2D materials, which are difficult to modify due to their lack of modifiable dangling bonds. The variable-valence metal ions in metalated COFs provide potential redox sites that allow controlled adjustment of sensing performance over a wide range by adjusting the metal type and content.
In 2021, Yu et al. reported COF-366-Fe grown on graphene aerogel which was used as an electrochemical gas sensor for NO detection.769 The glassy carbon electrode coated with COF-366-Fe/GA had a high effective area due to the porous structure of COF-366-Fe, which facilitated the donation of electrons from COF-366-Fe to form [COF-366-Fe(III)]+, thus promoting the chemisorption of NO on the electrode, culminating in the formation of NO3−via electrochemical oxidation. The electrons released during this process formed an electric current with the assistance of graphene aerogel, the intensity of which was positively correlated with NO concentration. In phosphate buffer (0.01 M, pH = 7.4), the linear range of the sensor was 0.14–400 μM with a detection limit of 30 nM. COF-366-Fe/GA successfully detected the release of NO from HUVEC cells induced by different concentrations of arginine.
Ultrasmall nanoparticles that are used as electrode surface modifiers can significantly enhance and amplify the electrochemical signal due to their high conductivity and large specific surface area, but they are limited by easy agglomeration. Recent works have demonstrated that the in situ growth of nanoparticles in COF matrices can prevent the aggregation of the nanoparticles and is an effective method of enhancing electrochemical detection. In 2022, the composite TFPB-BD-COF/PtNPs/NH2-MWCNT was obtained by Wang and co-workers via integrating Pt nanoparticles and carbon nanotubes on a TFPB-BD-COF matrix, and it enabled the simultaneous detection of three benzenediol isomers.353 Pt nanoparticle electrocatalysts catalysed the electrooxidation of the analytes, TFPB-BD-COF protected the Pt nanoparticles, and carbon nanotubes played a role in enhancing the electrical conductivity. In phosphate buffer solution, the TFPB-BD-COF/PtNPs/NH2-MWCNT was coated on the surface of a glassy carbon electrode and the benzenediols were oxidized to benzoquinones, resulting in the effective separation of the redox peaks of the three analytes. The response current of the differential pulse voltammetry scans was linearly related to the concentration of the analytes over wide concentration ranges, with detection limits of 15, 260, and 22 nM for 1,2-benzenediol, 1,3-benzenediol, and 1,4-benzenediol, respectively. In addition, the abundant nitrogen in squaraine-linked TS-COF provided binding sites for the immobilization of gold nanoparticles in the pores to build a composite AuNPs@TS-COF material.342 After the introduction of reduced graphene oxide, AuNPs@TS-COF/RGO could simultaneously detect uric acid, dopamine, and ascorbic acid via a two-electron/two-proton process in urine samples with high sensitivity and high reliability. Similarly, based on the above electrocatalytic oxidation principle, chlorogenic acid340 and bisphenol A770 could be quantified by COF-supported gold and palladium nanoparticles, respectively.
Based on Au-doped COFs, sophisticated electrochemical immunosensors can be designed to achieve ultrasensitive analysis of proteins. Gold nanoparticles not only increase the conductivity of the electrode but also provide binding sites to facilitate the adsorption of antibodies onto the electrode sheet so that antibody–antigen interactions can occur on the electrode surface. A sandwich-type electrochemical immunosensor was fabricated for the detection of kidney injury molecule-1 (KIM-1) using the gold nanoparticle-modified TAB-PTA COF as the electrochemical sensing platform and the porous NiCo2S4@CeO2 core–shell material as the signal amplifier.338 Under optimal conditions, the obtained immunosensor showed a good linear current signal response in the KIM-1 concentration range of 0.01–50 pg mL−1 with a low detection limit of 2.0 fg mL−1, maintaining stable detection results for 7 weeks. Furthermore, when horseradish peroxidase was incorporated as a signal amplifier, Au-modified DMTP-TAPB-COF was used to construct an enzymatic electroimmunosensor for cardiac troponin I.771 The response current of this sensor was linearly related to the logarithmic concentration of cardiac troponin I with a wide sensitivity range from 5 pg mL−1 to 10 ng mL−1 and a low detection limit of 1.7 pg mL−1. Glucose oxidase, hemoglobin, horseradish peroxidase, immunoglobulin G and cardiac troponin T did not interfere with the sensor, which could detect cardiac troponin I specifically in serum samples.
An electrochemical aptamer sensor based on metalated COFs was reported by Yuan et al.339 Single-stranded DNA was immobilized on an Fe3O4@COF-Au-coated glassy carbon electrode via Au–N bonds. Due to the interaction between the ATP aptamer and ATP, the chain of nucleic acids that was complementarily paired with the aptamer was dissociated, which in turn triggered the layered-branched hybridization chain reaction. The redox substrate (viz. 4-nitrophenol) was prevented from approaching the electrode, and electron transfer was confined, thus increasing the resistance of the device. In this way, the ATP concentration was linked to measurable electrochemical signals, achieving an extremely low detection limit of 1.6 pM and a wide linear range of 5–5 × 107 pM.
Electrochemiluminescence is a specific chemiluminescence phenomenon caused by electrochemical reactions on the electrode surface that combines the controllability of electrochemical methods and the sensitivity of chemiluminescence methods and has the advantages of low background, high sensitivity, and strong specificity.772,773 The Qiu group reported some COFs with electrochemiluminescence properties774,775 and their application in UO22+ detection.776,777
Briefly, electrochemical sensing has the advantages of sensitive response, simple operation, and low cost, but metal-free COFs have not been widely used in electrochemical sensing due to their poor electrical conductivity. By contrast, metal nanoparticle-loaded COFs to some extent cover the shortage of low conductivity and have been developed for electrochemical sensing, but there is still room for improvement in reproducibility and stability.


3.6.5. Surface-enhanced Raman scattering. 
The Raman scattering intensity of molecules adsorbed on the surface of precious metals such as Au and Ag is enhanced by several orders of magnitude, and this phenomenon is called surface-enhanced Raman scattering (SERS).778 Throughout the nearly 50-year development of this technique, substrates for SERS have usually been irregularly shaped colloidal nanoparticles, including nanostars, nanorods, nanocubes, and heterojunctions of noble metals.779 However, these colloidal nanoparticles have potential aggregation problems, which can usually be circumvented by embedding the particles in porous materials such as COFs. In fact, COFs not only serve as carriers for metal substrates to prevent undesired aggregation and deactivation but also provide a channel for molecular diffusion to effectively enrich the SERS reporter molecules and analytes, achieving the rapid, noninvasive, and specific detection of trace analytes.
In 2022, Chen et al. found that Ag-decorated TPB-DVA-COF can enrich benzoic acid on the surface.780 Due to the SERS effect, the Raman signal of the surface-adsorbed benzoic acid at 1003 cm−1 was greatly enhanced over that of unadsorbed benzoic acid, thereby improving the sensitivity of SERS detection. After optimization of the measuring conditions, the SERS signal intensity was linearly related to the benzoic acid concentration in the range of 0.05–1 μg mL−1, and the detection limit was 3.72 ng mL−1. Furthermore, gold nanostar-modified DMTP-TAPB-COF was shown to adsorb 4-nitrobenzenethiol through Au–S bonds and catalyze its reduction to 4-aminobenzenethiol by NaBH4, enhancing the Raman signals at 1591 cm−1 and reducing those at 1573 cm−1 in the SERS assay, leading to a ratiometric SERS signal.341 When monoclonal and polyclonal antibodies are combined with β-lactoglobulin, Au@DMTP-TAPB-COF can replace natural enzymes in classical enzyme-linked immunosorbent assays, achieving ratiometric ultrasensitive detection of β-lactoglobulin with an ultralow detection limit of 0.01 ng mL−1 within a linear range from 26 ng mL−1 to 62 μg mL−1.


3.6.6. Other advanced analytical methods. 
In addition to the above mentioned detection methods, metal-based nanoparticles supported by COFs also have potential applications in other detection techniques.
As reported by Jiang et al. in 2020, COF-LZU1 embedded with Ag nanoparticles can catalyse the reaction of CuSO4 with glucose to form Cu2O, which has a strong resonance Rayleigh scattering (RRS) signal at 370 nm.346 The strong adsorption of aptamers on the Ag@COF-LZU1 surface leads to the inhibition of its catalytic activity. The target molecule corresponding to the aptamer can form a complex with the aptamer, which leads to the release of the aptamer from the Ag@COF-LZU1 surface and restoration of the catalytic activity. Thus, the RRS signal intensity at 370 nm was correlated with the concentration of the target molecule with the help of the specific binding reaction between the aptamer and the target molecule on the Ag@COF-LZU1 surface. Through the use of different aptamer sequences, Ag@COF-LZU1 was successfully used for the RRS detection of urea, melamine, and bisphenol A.
Furthermore, metalated COFs have also been used to establish methods of analysing trace metal ions. In 2022, Wang et al. developed a MoS2-embedded TpPa-1 COF heterojunction for the accurate analysis of trace Ni(II) via photochemical vapor generation–microplasma optical emission spectrometry, with a detection limit of 0.03 μg L−1 and a linear range of 0.1–10 μg L−1.781 Cai et al. developed a quartz crystal microbalance sensor by growing the 2,2′-bipyridine-based Bpda-Bth COF on the surface of gold-coated quartz crystal chips, which achieved detection of trace Hg(II) at a detection limit of 58 nM within a linear range of 5–50 μM.782

3.7. Biomedicine
With the joint efforts of researchers in chemistry, materials science, cell biology, and clinical medicine, the application of nanomaterials in tumor therapeutics,783–785 antibacterial therapy,786,787 and tissue engineering788 has made great progress over the past three decades.

3.7.1. Oncotherapy. 
Nanomaterials have unique physical, chemical, and biological properties that provide new perspectives for the diagnosis, tracking, and treatment of tumors.789 For example, quantum dots are ultrasmall nanoparticles with semiconducting properties whose fluorescence emission can be adjusted by changing their size, shape, and chemical composition; they are used for tumor imaging in vivo.790–792 Mesoporous silica is widely studied as a carrier for chemotherapeutic drugs, and due to its passive targeting nature, the loaded drugs can achieve targeted sustained release in the tumor region, minimizing side effects.793,794 Hafnium-based materials with strong X-ray absorption are used as radiosensitizers to enhance the radiotherapeutic efficacy of tumors.795–797 Gold nanorods, copper sulfide, and MXenes can effectively convert light into heat energy when irradiated by near-infrared light, thus achieving tumor ablation through photothermal therapy (PTT).651–653,798 Fe3O4 nanoparticles effectively decompose H2O2 into highly toxic ˙OH in the tumor microenvironment and cause tumor cell death via apoptosis and ferroptosis; this method is known as chemodynamic therapy (CDT).799,800 Organic nanomaterials based on porphyrins, cyanines, and phthalocyanines can be excited by near-infrared light and ultrasonic waves and transfer the energy to oxygen to generate 1O2, which has extremely strong oxidative properties, inducing photodynamic therapy (PDT)801 and sonodynamic therapy (SDT).802,803 In addition, with nanoparticles as excipients, multiple therapeutic modalities can also be integrated into the same nanoplatform for combined therapy, which has the advantages of synergistic therapeutic mechanisms, reduced drug doses and enhanced treatment effects.804
COFs are latecomers, since they joined the nanooncology candidate library in the 2010s, but they have shown some advantages.117,714,805,806 COFs are highly diverse in terms of structure, particle size, charge, hydrophilicity, surface chemistry and other physicochemical properties,807 and thus, their biological interactions are not only manifold but also controllable, with great scope for customization.808 As nanoplatforms, COFs easily integrate multiple diagnostic and therapeutic strategies to achieve nanotheranostics.179 To date, COFs have been validated in the laboratory stage for drug delivery,809–818 phototherapy,819–825 immunotherapy,151,741,826–828 and combination therapy applications.817,829–832 In particular, the antitumor application of metalated COFs has been a promising research topic in COF-based nanomedicine. The types and contents of metal elements in metalated COFs can be adjusted as needed, which is advantageous for minimizing the potential long-term toxicity of metal elements.
In 2019, Pang et al. reported a Fe(III)-metalated Fe-TpPa-COF by simply mixing FeCl3 with TpPa-COF under ambient conditions.833 The resulting Fe-TpPa-COF showed no significant cytotoxicity even at concentrations up to 600 μg mL−1, implying negligible Fenton-like catalytic activity of Fe(III). Notably, Fe-TpPa-COF has a photothermal effect with a photothermal conversion efficiency of 13.9%. In cultured HeLa cells, 1.2 mg mL−1 of Fe-TpPa-COF resulted in a reduction in cell viability to approximately 30% under 808 nm laser irradiation at 1.8 W cm−2 for 5 min.
Iron-metalated COFs have also been used in CDT. An Fe(II)-metalated COF(Fe) was prepared by porphyrin-based TphDha-COF with FeCl2 in DMF, which was reported by Tang et al. in 2021 (Fig. 58A).834 Due to the porosity, the COF(Fe) was loaded with doxorubicin (DOX)—a clinical chemotherapeutic drug—to afford DOX@COF(Fe) with a DOX loading of 61.5 wt%. The highly dispersed Fe(II) sites in the COF matrix catalysed the Fenton-like reaction to convert H2O2 to ˙OH, inducing CDT. Furthermore, the generated ˙OH disrupted the efflux transporter p-glycoprotein on nuclear membranes and inhibited the efflux of DOX, thereby promoting nuclear accumulation and enhancing the chemotherapeutic effect of DOX. This result has been confirmed in vitro and in vivo with a DOX-resistant MCF-7 cell line and the corresponding xenograft model.
 Fig. 58  Fe(II)-Metalated COFs for cancer CDT. (A) CDT induced by ferrous porphyrin-based COFs for reversing tumor multidrug resistance. Adapted with permission of ref. 834. Copyright © 2021 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (B) Ferrocene-functionalized COFs for enhanced CDT by inhibiting glutathione peroxidase 4. Adapted with permission of ref. 149. Copyright © 2021 Wiley-VCH GmbH. 
The same year, the Dong group reported that the ferrocene-modified Tf-TAPB COF (namely COF-Fc) can induce CDT in HT-1080 fibrosarcoma, HCT-116 colorectal carcinoma, and MCF-7 breast carcinoma cells (Fig. 58B).149 After loading RSL3 in COF-Fc to inhibit glutathione peroxidase 4, RSL3@COF-Fc disrupted the intracellular antioxidant mechanism and led to an increase in intracellular lipid peroxide and subsequent ferroptosis.
In addition to being used for CDT, the Fenton-like reactivity of iron-metalated COFs can also be combined with chemiluminescent substrates to realize PDT excited by internal light sources, which greatly ameliorate the situation of insufficient penetration of external light sources into tumor tissue. Recently, Dong et al. synthesized a Fe(II)-metalated Fe-DhaTph COF for loading luminol, realizing in situ chemiluminescence-excited PDT.835 In H2O2-rich 4T1 solid tumors, Fe(II) catalyses the oxidation of luminol by H2O2 to produce blue chemiluminescence, thereby exciting porphyrins to generate 1O2 for PDT. By avoiding the use of clumsy external light sources, self-luminous excitation of PDT fundamentally circumvents the dilemma faced by phototherapy of deep tumors. This study demonstrated that Fe(II)-metalated COFs have potential in phototherapy of deep tumors.
Metalated COFs derived from copper porphyrins and copper phthalocyanines have been recently used as phototherapy agents. In 2021, Dong and co-workers reported a Cu-DhaTph COF based on copper(II) porphyrin; it has poor photodynamic properties due to the spin-forbidden d–d transition of Cu(II).836 Interestingly, the Cu-DhaTph COF can be triggered by endogenous H2S via an intracellular sulfidation reaction to generate a metal-free DhaTph COF as the photosensitizer for PDT against H2S-rich colon tumors with controllable singlet oxygen release; meanwhile in situ generated CuS can be synchronously used as a photothermal agent for PTT. This intracellular H2S-dependent demetalation resulted in combined PDT and PTT under 660 and 808 nm laser irradiation, which significantly inhibited colon cancer in vitro and in vivo.
Moreover, in 2021, a copper phthalocyanine-based CPF-Cu COF was synthesized by Zang and co-workers using 1,2,4,5-tetracyanobenzene as the monomer.222 Additional 3,6-dihydroxyphthalonitrile was added as a regulator to limit the extended growth within the layer and confine CPF-Cu to the nanoscale, and the hydroxyl group at the edge of the nanoparticles made CPF-Cu hydrophilic, both of which are beneficial for antitumor applications. The fused conjugated phthalocyanine framework restricts the rotation of intramolecular chemical bonds, which partially blocks the nonradiative transitions. Therefore, the CPF-Cu COF has green fluorescence emission at 520 nm with a fluorescence quantum yield of 10.3% in water upon excitation at 390 nm, which can be used for cell fluorescence imaging. Furthermore, the residual nonradiative energy decay can enable CPF-Cu to promote photothermal conversion. The photothermal conversion efficiency was 39.3% upon 660 nm laser irradiation when the CPF-Cu concentration was 100 μg mL−1. As expected, at low concentrations, CPF-Cu significantly inhibited the proliferation of SMMC7721 hepatoma cells when irradiated by a 660 nm laser. Mechanistic studies suggested that CPF-Cu-induced PTT downregulated the anti-apoptotic protein Blc-2, thereby inducing cell apoptosis.
Recently, Sun et al. reported the terpyridine–copper(II)-decorated COF-909-Cu by a direct coordination reaction of COF-909 with CuCl2 in methanol. COF-909-Cu has multiple functions in cells.151 First, COF-909-Cu promotes the cascade reaction of ˙O2− → H2O2 → ˙OH, which induces CDT. Second, COF-909-Cu oxidizes glutathione to oxidized glutathione and relieves the spontaneous cellular scavenging of reactive oxygen species. Finally, COF-909-Cu possesses photothermal conversion properties, and the induced temperature increase not only promotes the Fenton-like reaction and glutathione oxidation but also induces PTT. COF-909-Cu leads to caspase 3 activation, gasdermin E cleavage to generate N-terminal domains, and cell membrane perforation in 4T1 tumor cells; these phenomena are characteristic of pyroptosis. As expected, COF-909-Cu combined with an 808 nm laser almost completely cured 4T1 tumor-bearing mice. Furthermore, cell disruption was also accompanied by danger-associated molecular pattern release, which triggered antitumor immunity. COF-909-Cu combined with α-PD-1 immunotherapy induced long-lasting abscopal effects, thereby inhibiting tumor metastasis and recurrence.
Moreover, Ag(I)-metalated BpyTp-COF rapidly captured 125I within 30 s to obtain 125I-COF with 94% radiolabeling yield.233 In PC-3 tumor-bearing mice, the radiation intensity of 125I-COF at the tumor site was 3.2 times higher than that of 125I at 30 min after injection. At 24 h after injection, high radioactivity was still present at the tumor site injected with 125I-COF, while the radioactivity was no longer detectable in the tumor site injected with 125I. The retention time of 125I-COF in the tumor was much longer than that of 125I due to the interaction of the COFs with the tumor tissue, which facilitated radionuclide-based internal radiation therapy. Intratumoral injection of 125I-COF into PC-3 tumor-bearing mice at 4-day intervals resulted in significant tumor ablation, whereas there was no difference between 125I-injected and untreated mice.
In addition to the abovementioned metalated COFs formed by the coordination of metal ions with COFs, metal nanoparticle-embedded COFs are also applied in tumor therapy. Embedding photothermal nanoagents into COFs can prevent nanoparticle aggregation and improve the PTT effects. Two therapeutic nanomaterials obtained by Pang et al. via growing CuSe142 and Ag2Se378 within COF-LZU1 exhibited photothermal conversion efficiencies of 26.3 and 37.9%, respectively, upon 808 nm laser irradiation, which effectively induced tumor cell apoptosis and solid tumor ablation via PTT.
Due to the versatility of metalated COFs, multiple therapeutic agents can be integrated to realize multimodal combination therapy. According to the report of Dong et al., bifunctional CuS@COF-BDP nanomaterials were obtained by introducing ultrasmall CuS into TPB-DMTP-COF using copper acetate and thioacetamide as starting materials, followed by covalent modification of the amino groups on the TPB-DMTP-COF surface with a boron-dipyrromethene (BODIPY) photosensitizer.837 Upon 520 nm laser irradiation, CuS@COF-BDP induces 1O2 generation for PDT. Upon 1064 nm laser irradiation, CuS@COF-BDP leads to an increase in the temperature of the illuminated region for PTT because of its near-infrared light absorption and photothermal conversion ability. PDT and PTT synergistically cause significant lethality against MCF-7 breast cancer in vitro and in vivo.
Gold nanomaterials are considered as promising multifunctional therapeutic agents due to their photothermal properties and catalytic activity.838 Gold nanomaterial-loaded COFs are potential multimodal therapeutic platforms that are easy to further functionalize. As reported by the Tang group in 2021, CApHD nanosheets were obtained by in situ growth of gold nanoparticles on a photosensitive porphyrin-COF, followed by loading with DOX and covalent modification with a pH-responsive insertion peptide.839 As expected, CApHD induced the upregulation of reactive oxygen species and local overheating upon 635 nm laser irradiation, which disrupted cell membrane integrity and promoted DOX release and entry into tumor cells, ultimately enabling cell membrane-targeted synergetic PDT, PTT and chemotherapy. In addition, in 2021, Lin et al. prepared a COF-Au-MnO2-HA composite with catalytic cascade reaction activity by growing gold nanoparticles and MnO2 sequentially on TPB-DMTP-COF for T1-weighted magnetic resonance imaging and combination therapy of hypoxic tumors.326 The authors demonstrated that the gold nanoparticles catalyze the glucose oxidation to generate gluconic acid and H2O2 and MnO2 subsequently decomposes the generated H2O2 into O2 for enhancing PDT.
In addition to CuS and Au, other types of nanomaterials embedded in COFs have also been reported. Recently, CaCO3-loaded COFs have been used for calcium overload-induced tumor therapy.375,840 As reported by Dong et al.,375 using the reaction of CaCl2 and NH4HCO3, CaCO3@COF-BODIPY-2I was prepared by the in situ formation of ultrasmall CaCO3 in TPB-DMTP-COF modified with a diiodo-substituted BODIPY photosensitizer (Fig. 59).375 Surface modification with glycosaminoglycan further improve the targeting ability of the nanomaterial to tumor cells with high CD44 expression. The obtained CaCO3@COF-BODIPY-2I@GAG induced 1O2 production under green LED light, leading to damage to mitochondria as intracellular calcium buffer pools. In the absence of the calcium buffering capacity of mitochondria, an irreversible defect in ion homeostasis is induced by elevated Ca(II) concentrations due to CaCO3 decomposition in acidic lysosomes, thereby leading to cell death. In the HCT-116 xenograft model, a single injection of CaCO3@COF-BODIPY-2I@GAG and subsequent light exposure almost eradicated the tumor, which showed no obvious signs of recurrence within 12 days. This study shows for the first time the use of metalated COFs in ion interference therapy and highlights the disruptive effect of PDT on cellular homeostasis.
 Fig. 59  Metalated COFs for ion interference therapy. (A) Synthesis of CaCO3@COF-BODIPY-2I@GAG. (B) CaCO3@COF-BODIPY-2I@GAG-induced Ca(II) overload and PDT. (C) HCT-116 cell morphological changes caused by 1O2 and Ca(II). Adapted with permission of ref. 375. Copyright © 2020 Wiley-VCH GmbH. 
Moreover, the band gap change caused by the composition of TiO2 with COFs may be beneficial for the generation of reactive oxygen species. In 2021, Pang et al. reported that a TiO2-loaded COF nanoagent, which was used for SDT, could be prepared by depositing anatase-phase TiO2 on the DMTP-TAPB-COF surface.363 The energy band gap of COF-TiO2 decreased from 3.15 eV to 2.42 eV compared to pure TiO2. The narrow band gap was beneficial for improving the electron–hole separation efficiency and enhancing 1O2 generation under sonication. To enhance biocompatibility, COF-TiO2 was further modified with hyaluronic acid. In cultured 4T1 cancer cells, the resulting COF-TiO2-HA induced the upregulation of intracellular reactive oxygen species and led to cell death in a concentration-dependent manner under sonication with a frequency of 1.0 MHz and a power density of 1.5 W cm−2. In an in vivo model of 4T1 tumors, COF-TiO2-HA induced apoptosis and inhibited tumor growth. This is the first report of sonosensitized COFs, and it has greatly inspired the study of COF-based SDT. Finally, COF coatings of COF-TiO2-HA can improve pharmacokinetics, increase tumor accumulation, and optimize therapeutic efficacy in tumor-bearing mice.
Overall, research on the oncotherapeutic applications of metalated COFs is still at a very early stage and many persistent challenges and limitations must be considered. First, due to the size limitations of mammalian cells for uptake of therapeutic nanomaterials, which is generally considered to be an upper limit of 200 nm, the morphological controls during the preparation of metalated COFs cannot be neglected. The dispersibility and colloidal stability of metalated COFs in aqueous solutions are one of the major bottlenecks limiting their biologically relevant applications, especially oncotherapy applications. Second, although the current study implies that the acute toxicity of metalated COFs in rodents is negligible, the metabolic process and long-term toxicity of metalated COFs in vivo have not been studied. Third, in theory, metal coordination can change the interaction between the guest and the framework, thereby regulating the uptake and release of the guest, but the significance of such precise regulation in metalated COF-mediated drug delivery has not been given much attention.


3.7.2. Antimicrobials. 
Infections caused by bacteria represent an important clinical challenge. Compared to tumor cells, bacteria have additional protective membranes (e.g., outer membrane, capsular polysaccharide layer, and cytoderm)841 and tend to form biofilms to escape the effects of antimicrobial agents and host immune responses.842 The overuse and misuse of conventional antibiotics lead to the development of bacteria toward drug-resistant or even multidrug-resistant bacteria, which is a global public health crisis of growing proportions.843 Due to these intrinsic and extrinsic difficulties, the development of bionanomaterial-based antimicrobial therapeutics is an alternative to conventional antibiotics that is gaining attention.787,844 COF-based antimicrobials are highly customizable biomaterials. COFs with intrinsic antibacterial activity can be easily designed and synthesized by introducing antibacterial moieties, including guanidine,845,846 propidium iodide,847 and benzooxazine.848 Additionally, due to their porosity, COFs can be loaded with high levels of antibiotics to provide slow and sustained antibiotic release.849–851 Furthermore, photosensitive COFs induce the generation of reactive oxygen species (including 1O2, ˙O2−, ˙OH, etc.) under light, causing the oxidation of bacterial membrane structures and killing bacteria.852–857 Additionally, COFs can be composited with other antimicrobial nanomaterials, such as Fe-based MOFs,858 MXenes,859 and nanosilver,860 to enhance the adhesion of the biomaterials on the bacterial surface and improve the antimicrobial activity.
Fe-metalated COFs can induce bacterial death by virtue of the chemodynamic activity of Fe(II). In 2021, Xiang et al. reported an antibacterial GFeF material that was synthesized by loading glucose oxidase on iron-metalated Fe-iCOF (Fig. 60).150 The loaded glucose oxidase converted glucose to gluconic acid and H2O2, and then the H2O2 decomposed to ˙OH under the catalysis of Fe-iCOF. The activity of Fe-iCOF was significantly increased in the acidic environment due to gluconic acid. Eventually, the vast accumulation of ˙OH leads to membrane perforation and death of E. coli and S. aureus. Interestingly, the addition of GFeF to a mixture of β-glycerophosphate and chitosan allowed the synthesis of thermosensitive GFeF hydrogels, which inhibited S. aureus infection, promoted vascular endothelial growth factor expression, and reduced inflammatory cell infiltration when used as wound dressings, thereby accelerating wound healing.
 Fig. 60  Fe-iCOF as an efficient cascade catalyst for combating bacterial infection and promoting wound healing. (A) Schematic diagram of the cascade reaction.
